<p><b>Jesus</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[e]</a></sup> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="/dʒ/: 'j' in 'jam'">dʒ</span><span title="/iː/: 'ee' in 'fleece'">iː</span><span title="'z' in 'zoom'">z</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span></span>/</a></span></span> <a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><small title="English pronunciation respelling"><i><b>JEE</b>-zuss</i></small></a>; <a href="/wiki/Circa" title="Circa">c.</a><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 4 BC</span> – c.<span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 30/33 AD</span>), also referred to as <b>Jesus of Nazareth</b> and <b>Jesus Christ</b>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[f]</a></sup> was a <a href="/wiki/Jews" title="Jews">Jewish</a> preacher and religious leader<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vermes_1981_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vermes_1981-18">[12]</a></sup> who is the central figure of <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a>. <a href="/wiki/Christian" title="Christian">Christians</a> believe him to be the <a href="/wiki/Son_of_God_(Christianity)" title="Son of God (Christianity)">Son of God</a> and the awaited <a href="/wiki/Messiah#Christianity" title="Messiah">Messiah</a> (<a href="/wiki/Christ_(title)" title="Christ (title)">Christ</a>) prophesied in the <a href="/wiki/Old_Testament" title="Old Testament">Old Testament</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcGrath20064.E2.80.936_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcGrath20064.E2.80.936-19">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ehrman_Jesus_became_God_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ehrman_Jesus_became_God-20">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>Virtually all modern scholars of antiquity agree that <a href="/wiki/Historicity_of_Jesus" title="Historicity of Jesus">Jesus existed historically</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-exist_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-exist-27">[g]</a></sup> although the <a href="/wiki/Quest_for_the_historical_Jesus" title="Quest for the historical Jesus">quest for the historical Jesus</a> has produced little agreement on the <a href="/wiki/Historical_reliability_of_the_Gospels" title="Historical reliability of the Gospels">historical reliability of the Gospels</a> and on how closely the biblical Jesus reflects the <a href="/wiki/Historical_Jesus" title="Historical Jesus">historical Jesus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPowell1998168.E2.80.9373_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPowell1998168.E2.80.9373-28">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[h]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[i]</a></sup> Jesus was a <a href="/wiki/Galilean" title="Galilean">Galilean</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jew" title="Jew">Jew</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vermes_1981_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-Vermes_1981-18">[12]</a></sup> who was <a href="/wiki/Baptism_of_Jesus" title="Baptism of Jesus">baptized</a> by <a href="/wiki/John_the_Baptist" title="John the Baptist">John the Baptist</a> and subsequently began <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Jesus" title="Ministry of Jesus">his own ministry</a>, preaching his message <a href="/wiki/Oral_gospel_traditions" title="Oral gospel traditions">orally</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2013_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dunn2013-33">[24]</a></sup> and often being referred to as "<a href="/wiki/Rabbi" title="Rabbi">rabbi</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ISBEO_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-ISBEO-34">[25]</a></sup> Jesus debated fellow Jews on how to best follow God, engaged in healings, taught in <a href="/wiki/Parable" title="Parable">parables</a> and gathered followers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELevine20064_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELevine20064-35">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[27]</a></sup> He was arrested and tried by the <a href="/wiki/Sanhedrin" title="Sanhedrin">Jewish authorities</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESanders199311_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESanders199311-37">[28]</a></sup> and turned over to the Roman government, and was subsequently <a href="/wiki/Crucifixion_of_Jesus" title="Crucifixion of Jesus">crucified</a> on the order of <a href="/wiki/Pontius_Pilate" title="Pontius Pilate">Pontius Pilate</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Roman_governor" title="Roman governor">Roman prefect</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELevine20064_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELevine20064-35">[26]</a></sup> After his death, his followers believed he <a href="/wiki/Resurrection_of_Jesus" title="Resurrection of Jesus">rose from the dead</a>, and the community they formed eventually became the <a href="/wiki/Christian_Church" title="Christian Church">Christian Church</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESanders199311.2C_14_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESanders199311.2C_14-38">[29]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Nativity_of_Jesus" title="Nativity of Jesus">His birth</a> is celebrated annually on December 25 (or various dates in January for some eastern churches) as a holiday known as <a href="/wiki/Christmas" title="Christmas">Christmas</a>, his crucifixion is honored on <a href="/wiki/Good_Friday" title="Good Friday">Good Friday</a>, and his resurrection is celebrated on <a href="/wiki/Easter" title="Easter">Easter</a>. The widely used <a href="/wiki/Calendar_era" title="Calendar era">calendar era</a> "<a href="/wiki/Anno_Domini" title="Anno Domini">AD</a>", from the Latin <i>anno Domini</i> ("in the year of the Lord"), and the alternative "<a href="/wiki/Common_Era" title="Common Era">CE</a>", are based on the approximate birth date of Jesus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AD_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-AD-39">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[j]</a></sup></p>
<p>Christian doctrines include the beliefs that Jesus was conceived by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Holy_Spirit_(Christianity)" title="Holy Spirit (Christianity)">Holy Spirit</a>, was <a href="/wiki/Virgin_birth_of_Jesus" title="Virgin birth of Jesus">born of a virgin</a> named <a href="/wiki/Mary,_mother_of_Jesus" title="Mary, mother of Jesus">Mary</a>, performed <a href="/wiki/Miracles_of_Jesus" title="Miracles of Jesus">miracles</a>, founded the Church, died by crucifixion as a sacrifice to achieve <a href="/wiki/Atonement_in_Christianity" title="Atonement in Christianity">atonement</a>, rose from the dead, and <a href="/wiki/Ascension_of_Jesus" title="Ascension of Jesus">ascended</a> into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heaven_(Christianity)" title="Heaven (Christianity)">Heaven</a>, from where he <a href="/wiki/Second_Coming" title="Second Coming">will return</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrudem1994568.E2.80.93603_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrudem1994568.E2.80.93603-42">[32]</a></sup> Most <a href="/wiki/Jesus_in_Christianity" title="Jesus in Christianity">Christians believe Jesus</a> enables humans to be reconciled to God. The <a href="/wiki/Nicene_Creed" title="Nicene Creed">Nicene Creed</a> asserts that Jesus will <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Last_Judgement" title="Last Judgement">judge the living and the dead</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[33]</a></sup> either <a href="/wiki/Intermediate_state" title="Intermediate state">before</a> or <a href="/wiki/Christian_mortalism" title="Christian mortalism">after</a> their <a href="/wiki/Resurrection_of_the_dead#Christianity" title="Resurrection of the dead">bodily resurrection</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oxford_Companion_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Oxford_Companion-44">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[36]</a></sup> an event tied to the Second Coming of Jesus in <a href="/wiki/Christian_eschatology" title="Christian eschatology">Christian eschatology</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[37]</a></sup> The great majority of Christians worship Jesus as the <a href="/wiki/Incarnation_(Christianity)" title="Incarnation (Christianity)">incarnation</a> of <a href="/wiki/God_the_Son" title="God the Son">God the Son</a>, the second of three <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Person_(theology)#Christian_theology" title="Person (theology)">persons</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Trinity" title="Trinity">Divine Trinity</a>. A minority of <a href="/wiki/Christian_denomination" title="Christian denomination">Christian denominations</a> <a href="/wiki/Nontrinitarianism" title="Nontrinitarianism">reject Trinitarianism</a>, wholly or partly, as non-scriptural.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Jesus_in_Islam" title="Jesus in Islam">In Islam</a>, Jesus (commonly transliterated as <span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="ISO 233 Arabic"><a href="/wiki/Isa_(name)" title="Isa (name)"><i>Isa</i></a></span>) is considered one of <a href="/wiki/God_in_Islam" title="God in Islam">God</a>'s important <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prophets_in_Islam" title="Prophets in Islam">prophets</a> and the Messiah.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-uscsite_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-uscsite-48">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CEI_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-CEI-49">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Siddiqui_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Siddiqui-50">[40]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">Muslims</a> believe Jesus was a <a href="/wiki/Prophets_and_messengers_in_Islam" title="Prophets and messengers in Islam">bringer of scripture</a> and was born of a virgin but was not the Son of God. The <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quran</a> states that Jesus himself never claimed divinity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morgan_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Morgan-51">[41]</a></sup> To most Muslims, Jesus <a href="/wiki/Islamic_view_of_Jesus%27_death" title="Islamic view of Jesus' death">was not crucified</a> but was physically <a href="/wiki/Entering_Heaven_alive" title="Entering Heaven alive">raised into Heaven</a> by God.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Judaism%27s_view_of_Jesus" title="Judaism's view of Jesus">Judaism rejects</a> the belief that Jesus was the awaited Messiah, arguing that he did not fulfill <a href="/wiki/Messiah_in_Judaism" title="Messiah in Judaism">Messianic prophecies</a> and asserting that his resurrection is a <a href="/wiki/Christian_mythology" title="Christian mythology">Christian legend</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JE1906_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-JE1906-52">[42]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Jesus_(name)" title="Jesus (name)">Jesus (name)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Holy_Name_of_Jesus" title="Holy Name of Jesus">Holy Name of Jesus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Names_and_titles_of_Jesus_in_the_New_Testament" title="Names and titles of Jesus in the New Testament">Names and titles of Jesus in the New Testament</a>, <a href="/wiki/Names_of_God_in_Christianity" title="Names of God in Christianity">Names of God in Christianity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yeshua" title="Yeshua">Yeshua</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Isa_(name)" title="Isa (name)">Isa (name)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:JesusYeshua2.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="348" data-file-width="819" height="93" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/JesusYeshua2.svg/220px-JesusYeshua2.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/JesusYeshua2.svg/330px-JesusYeshua2.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/JesusYeshua2.svg/440px-JesusYeshua2.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:JesusYeshua2.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Counter-clockwise: Hebrew, Aramaic, Greek, Latin, and English transcriptions of the name <i>Jesus</i></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A typical <a href="/wiki/Jews" title="Jews">Jew</a> in Jesus' time <a href="/wiki/Jewish_name" title="Jewish name">had only one name</a>, sometimes <a href="/wiki/Patronymic" title="Patronymic">supplemented with the father's name</a> or the individual's hometown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-53">[43]</a></sup> Thus, in the New Testament, Jesus is commonly referred to as "Jesus of Nazareth"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[k]</a></sup> (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Mark_10" title="Mark 10">Mark 10</a>:47).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[44]</a></sup> Jesus' neighbors in Nazareth refer to him as "the carpenter, the son of Mary and brother of James and Joses and Judas and Simon" (<a href="/wiki/Mark_6" title="Mark 6">Mark 6</a>:3),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[45]</a></sup> "the carpenter's son" (<a href="/wiki/Matthew_13" title="Matthew 13">Matthew 13</a>:55),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[46]</a></sup> or "Joseph's son" (<a href="/wiki/Luke_4" title="Luke 4">Luke 4</a>:22).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[47]</a></sup> In John, the disciple <a href="/wiki/Philip_the_Apostle" title="Philip the Apostle">Philip</a> refers to him as "Jesus son of Joseph from Nazareth" (<a href="/wiki/John_1" title="John 1">John 1</a>:45).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[48]</a></sup></p>
<p>The name <i>Jesus</i> is derived from the Latin <i>Iesus</i>, a <a href="/wiki/Transliteration" title="Transliteration">transliteration</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> <span lang="grc">Ἰησοῦς</span> (<i><span lang="grc-Latn">Iesous</span></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CE_name_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-CE_name-60">[49]</a></sup> The Greek form is a rendering of the <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">Hebrew</a> <span dir="rtl" lang="arc">ישוע</span>‎ (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yeshua_(name)" title="Yeshua (name)">Yeshua</a></i>), a variant of the earlier name <span dir="rtl" lang="arc">יהושע</span>‎ (<i><a href="/wiki/Joshua_(name)" title="Joshua (name)">Yehoshua</a></i>), in English "Joshua".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EhrmanDid29_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-EhrmanDid29-62">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[52]</a></sup> The name <i>Yeshua</i> appears to have been in use in Judea at the time of the birth of Jesus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[53]</a></sup> The 1st century works of historian <a href="/wiki/Josephus" title="Josephus">Flavius Josephus</a>, who wrote in <a href="/wiki/Koine_Greek" title="Koine Greek">Koine Greek</a>, the same language as that of the New Testament,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[54]</a></sup> refer to at least twenty different people with the name Jesus (i.e. Ἰησοῦς).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEddyBoyd2007129_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEddyBoyd2007129-66">[55]</a></sup> The etymology of Jesus' name in the context of the New Testament is generally given as "Yahweh is salvation".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrance200753_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrance200753-67">[56]</a></sup></p>
<p>Since early Christianity, Christians have commonly referred to Jesus as "Jesus Christ".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoninger1999212_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoninger1999212-68">[57]</a></sup> The word <i>Christ</i> is derived from the Greek <span lang="grc"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%CE%A7%CF%81%CE%B9%CF%83%CF%84%CF%8C%CF%82" title="wikt:Χριστός">Χριστός</a></span> (<i>Christos</i>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CE_name_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-CE_name-60">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[58]</a></sup> which is a translation of the Hebrew <span lang="he"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%D7%9E%D6%B8%D7%A9%D6%B4%D7%81%D7%99%D7%97%D6%B7" title="wikt:מָשִׁיחַ">משיח</a></span> (<i>Meshiakh</i>), meaning the "<a href="/wiki/Anointing" title="Anointing">anointed</a>" and usually transliterated into English as "<a href="/wiki/Messiah" title="Messiah">Messiah</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVine1940274.E2.80.9375_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVine1940274.E2.80.9375-71">[60]</a></sup> Christians designate Jesus as Christ because they believe he is the Messiah, whose arrival is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jesus_and_messianic_prophecy" title="Jesus and messianic prophecy">prophesied</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_Bible" title="Hebrew Bible">Hebrew Bible</a> and Old Testament. In postbiblical usage, <i>Christ</i> became viewed as a name—one part of "Jesus Christ"—but originally it was a title.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPannenberg196830.E2.80.9331_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPannenberg196830.E2.80.9331-72">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[62]</a></sup> The term "<a href="/wiki/Christian" title="Christian">Christian</a>" (meaning a follower of Christ) has been in use since the 1st century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMillsBullard1998.5Bhttps:.2F.2Fbooks.google.com.2Fbooks.3Fid.3Dgoq0VWw9rGIC.26lpg.3DPA142.26pg.3DPA142.23v.3Donepage.26q.3D142.2520allegiance.26f.3Dfalse_142.5D_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMillsBullard1998.5Bhttps:.2F.2Fbooks.google.com.2Fbooks.3Fid.3Dgoq0VWw9rGIC.26lpg.3DPA142.26pg.3DPA142.23v.3Donepage.26q.3D142.2520allegiance.26f.3Dfalse_142.5D-74">[63]</a></sup></p>

</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Canonical_gospels">Canonical gospels</span></h3>
<p>The four <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canonical_gospel" title="Canonical gospel">canonical gospels</a> (Matthew, Mark, Luke, and <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_John" title="Gospel of John">John</a>) are the foremost sources for the life and message of Jesus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-53">[43]</a></sup> However, other parts of the New Testament also include references to key episodes in his life, such as the <a href="/wiki/Last_Supper" title="Last Supper">Last Supper</a> in 1 Corinthians 11:23.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlomberg2009441.E2.80.9342_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlomberg2009441.E2.80.9342-75">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fahlbusch52_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fahlbusch52-76">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans2003465.E2.80.93477_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans2003465.E2.80.93477-77">[66]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_the_Apostles" title="Acts of the Apostles">Acts of the Apostles</a> (Acts 10:37–38 and Acts 19) refers to the early ministry of Jesus and its anticipation by John the Baptist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bruce1988_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bruce1988-78">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERausch200377_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERausch200377-79">[68]</a></sup> Acts 1:1–11 says more about the Ascension of Jesus (also mentioned in 1 Timothy 3:16) than the canonical gospels do.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans2003521.E2.80.9330_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans2003521.E2.80.9330-80">[69]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Pauline_epistles#Authenticity" title="Pauline epistles">undisputed Pauline letters</a>, which were written earlier than the gospels, the words or instructions of Jesus are cited several times (1 Corinthians 7:10–11, 9:14, 11:23–25, 2 Corinthians 12:9).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[l]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some <a href="/wiki/Early_Christianity" title="Early Christianity">early Christian</a> groups had separate descriptions of the life and teachings of Jesus that are not included in the New Testament. These include the Gospels of <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_Thomas" title="Gospel of Thomas">Thomas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_Peter" title="Gospel of Peter">Peter</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_Judas" title="Gospel of Judas">Judas</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Apocryphon_of_James" title="Apocryphon of James">Apocryphon of James</a>, and <a href="/wiki/New_Testament_apocrypha" title="New Testament apocrypha">many other apocryphal writings</a>. Most scholars conclude that these are written much later and are less reliable accounts than the canonical gospels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrown1997835.E2.80.9340_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrown1997835.E2.80.9340-83">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[73]</a></sup></p>
<p>The canonical gospels are four accounts, each written by a different author. The authors of the gospels are all anonymous, attributed by tradition to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Four_evangelists" title="Four evangelists">four evangelists</a>, each with close ties to Jesus:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5GIntro_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-5GIntro-86">[74]</a></sup> Mark by <a href="/wiki/John_Mark" title="John Mark">John Mark</a>, an associate of Peter;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-May_Metzger_Mark_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-May_Metzger_Mark-87">[75]</a></sup> Matthew by one of Jesus' disciples;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5GIntro_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-5GIntro-86">[74]</a></sup> Luke by a companion of Paul mentioned in a few epistles;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5GIntro_86-2"><a href="#cite_note-5GIntro-86">[74]</a></sup> and John by another of Jesus' disciples,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5GIntro_86-3"><a href="#cite_note-5GIntro-86">[74]</a></sup> the "beloved disciple".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrossLivingstone2005John.2C_St._88-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrossLivingstone2005John.2C_St.-88">[76]</a></sup></p>
<p>One important aspect of the study of the gospels is the <a href="/wiki/Literary_genre" title="Literary genre">literary genre</a> under which they fall. Genre "is a key convention guiding both the composition and the interpretation of writings".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[77]</a></sup> Whether the gospel authors set out to write novels, myths, histories, or biographies has a tremendous impact on how they ought to be interpreted. Some recent studies suggest that the genre of the gospels ought to be situated within the realm of ancient <a href="/wiki/Biography" title="Biography">biography</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[80]</a></sup> Although not without critics,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[81]</a></sup> the position that the gospels are a type of ancient biography is the consensus among scholars today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[83]</a></sup></p>
<p>Not everything contained in the New Testament gospels is considered to be historically reliable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESanders19933_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESanders19933-96">[84]</a></sup> Views range from their being <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inerrancy" title="Inerrancy">inerrant</a> descriptions of the life of Jesus<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrudem199490.E2.80.9391_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrudem199490.E2.80.9391-97">[85]</a></sup> to their providing little historical information about his life beyond the basics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEK.C3.B6stenbergerKellumQuarles2009117.E2.80.9325_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEK.C3.B6stenbergerKellumQuarles2009117.E2.80.9325-98">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEhrman199922.E2.80.9323_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEhrman199922.E2.80.9323-99">[87]</a></sup> According to a broad scholarly consensus, the <a href="/wiki/Synoptic_Gospels" title="Synoptic Gospels">Synoptic Gospels</a> (Matthew, Mark, and Luke), and not John, are the most reliable sources of information about Jesus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESanders199371_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESanders199371-100">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETheissenMerz199817.E2.80.9362_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETheissenMerz199817.E2.80.9362-101">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_53-2"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-53">[43]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to the <a href="/wiki/Marcan_priority" title="Marcan priority">Marcan priority</a>, the first to be written was the Gospel of Mark (written AD 60–75), followed by the Gospel of Matthew (AD 65–85), the Gospel of Luke (AD 65–95), and the Gospel of John (AD 75–100).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[90]</a></sup> Furthermore, most scholars agree that the authors of Matthew and Luke used Mark as a source when writing their gospels. Matthew and Luke also share some content not found in Mark. To explain this, many scholars believe that in addition to Mark, another source (commonly called the "<a href="/wiki/Q_source" title="Q source">Q source</a>") was used by the two authors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELicona2010210.E2.80.9321_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELicona2010210.E2.80.9321-103">[91]</a></sup></p>
<p>Matthew, Mark, and Luke are known as the Synoptic Gospels, from the Greek σύν (<i>syn</i> "together") and ὄψις (<i>opsis</i> "view").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Synoptic_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-Synoptic-104">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Synoptic2_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-Synoptic2-105">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[94]</a></sup> They are similar in content, narrative arrangement, language and paragraph structure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Synoptic_104-1"><a href="#cite_note-Synoptic-104">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Synoptic2_105-1"><a href="#cite_note-Synoptic2-105">[93]</a></sup> Scholars generally agree that it is impossible to find any direct literary relationship between the Synoptic Gospels and the Gospel of John.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moloney3_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-Moloney3-107">[95]</a></sup> While the flow of some events (such as Jesus' baptism, <a href="/wiki/Transfiguration_of_Jesus" title="Transfiguration of Jesus">transfiguration</a>, crucifixion and interactions with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apostle_(Christian)" title="Apostle (Christian)">apostles</a>) are shared among the Synoptic Gospels, incidents such as the transfiguration do not appear in John, which also differs on other matters, such as the <a href="/wiki/Cleansing_of_the_Temple" title="Cleansing of the Temple">Cleansing of the Temple</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[96]</a></sup></p>
<table class="wikitable" style="font-size: 89%">
<tr>

</tr>

</table>
<p>The Synoptics emphasize different aspects of Jesus. In Mark, Jesus is the <a href="/wiki/Son_of_God_(Christianity)" title="Son of God (Christianity)">Son of God</a> whose mighty works demonstrate the presence of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/God%27s_Kingdom" title="God's Kingdom">God's Kingdom</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-May_Metzger_Mark_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-May_Metzger_Mark-87">[75]</a></sup> He is a tireless wonder worker, the servant of both God and man.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ThompsonPortraits_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-ThompsonPortraits-111">[99]</a></sup> This short gospel records few of Jesus' words or teachings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-May_Metzger_Mark_87-2"><a href="#cite_note-May_Metzger_Mark-87">[75]</a></sup> The Gospel of Matthew emphasizes that Jesus is the fulfillment of God's will as revealed in the Old Testament, and he is the Lord of the Church.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-May_Metzger_Matthew_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-May_Metzger_Matthew-112">[100]</a></sup> He is the "<a href="/wiki/Davidic_line" title="Davidic line">Son of David</a>", a "king", and the Messiah.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ThompsonPortraits_111-1"><a href="#cite_note-ThompsonPortraits-111">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcGrath20064.E2.80.936_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcGrath20064.E2.80.936-19">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ehrman_Jesus_became_God_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ehrman_Jesus_became_God-20">[14]</a></sup> Luke presents Jesus as the divine-human savior who shows compassion to the needy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-May_Metzger_Luke_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-May_Metzger_Luke-113">[101]</a></sup> He is the friend of sinners and outcasts, come to seek and save the lost.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ThompsonPortraits_111-2"><a href="#cite_note-ThompsonPortraits-111">[99]</a></sup> This gospel includes Jesus' most beloved parables, such as the Good Samaritan and the Prodigal Son.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-May_Metzger_Luke_113-1"><a href="#cite_note-May_Metzger_Luke-113">[101]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/John_1:1" title="John 1:1">prologue to the Gospel of John</a> identifies Jesus as an incarnation of the divine Word (<a href="/wiki/Logos_(Christianity)" title="Logos (Christianity)">Logos</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MayMetzgerJohn_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-MayMetzgerJohn-114">[102]</a></sup> As the Word, Jesus was eternally present with God, active in all creation, and the source of humanity's moral and spiritual nature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MayMetzgerJohn_114-1"><a href="#cite_note-MayMetzgerJohn-114">[102]</a></sup> Jesus is not only greater than any past human prophet but greater than any prophet could be. He not only speaks God's Word; he is God's Word.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarris1985302.E2.80.9310_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarris1985302.E2.80.9310-115">[103]</a></sup> In the Gospel of John, Jesus reveals his divine role publicly. Here he is the Bread of Life, the Light of the World, the True Vine and more.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ThompsonPortraits_111-3"><a href="#cite_note-ThompsonPortraits-111">[99]</a></sup></p>
<p>In general, the authors of the New Testament showed little interest in an absolute chronology of Jesus or in synchronizing the episodes of his life with the secular history of the age.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERahner2004730.E2.80.9331_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERahner2004730.E2.80.9331-116">[104]</a></sup> As stated in <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(American_Standard)/John#21:25" title="s:Bible (American Standard)/John">John 21:25</a>, the gospels do not claim to provide an exhaustive list of the events in the life of Jesus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[105]</a></sup> The accounts were primarily written as theological documents in the context of <a href="/wiki/Early_Christianity" title="Early Christianity">early Christianity</a>, with timelines as a secondary consideration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wiarda75_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wiarda75-118">[106]</a></sup> In this respect, it is noteworthy that the Gospels devote about one third of their text to the last week of the life of Jesus in <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem" title="Jerusalem">Jerusalem</a>, referred to as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Passion_(Christianity)" title="Passion (Christianity)">the Passion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Turner613_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-Turner613-119">[107]</a></sup> Although the gospels do not provide enough details to satisfy the demands of modern historians regarding exact dates, it is possible to draw from them a general picture of the life story of Jesus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESanders19933_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESanders19933-96">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERahner2004730.E2.80.9331_116-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERahner2004730.E2.80.9331-116">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wiarda75_118-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wiarda75-118">[106]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Genealogy_and_nativity">Genealogy and nativity</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Genealogy_of_Jesus" title="Genealogy of Jesus">Genealogy of Jesus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nativity_of_Jesus" title="Nativity of Jesus">Nativity of Jesus</a></div>
<p>Jesus was Jewish,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vermes_1981_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-Vermes_1981-18">[12]</a></sup> born by Mary, wife of <a href="/wiki/Saint_Joseph" title="Saint Joseph">Joseph</a> (<span class="nourlexpansion nowrap"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Matthew+1&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">Matthew 1</a></span>, <span class="nourlexpansion nowrap"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Luke+2&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">Luke 2</a></span>). Matthew and Luke each offer a <a href="/wiki/Genealogy_of_Jesus" title="Genealogy of Jesus">genealogy of Jesus</a>. Matthew traces Jesus' ancestry to <a href="/wiki/Abraham" title="Abraham">Abraham</a> through <a href="/wiki/David" title="David">David</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESanders199380.E2.80.9391_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESanders199380.E2.80.9391-120">[108]</a></sup> Luke traces Jesus' ancestry through <a href="/wiki/Adam" title="Adam">Adam</a> to God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrown1978163_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrown1978163-121">[109]</a></sup> The lists are identical between Abraham and David, but differ radically from that point.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gerard_van_Honthorst_-_Adoration_of_the_Shepherds_(1622).jpg"><img alt="A Nativity scene; men and animals surround Mary and newborn Jesus, who are covered in light" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1690" data-file-width="2057" height="181" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Gerard_van_Honthorst_-_Adoration_of_the_Shepherds_%281622%29.jpg/220px-Gerard_van_Honthorst_-_Adoration_of_the_Shepherds_%281622%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Gerard_van_Honthorst_-_Adoration_of_the_Shepherds_%281622%29.jpg/330px-Gerard_van_Honthorst_-_Adoration_of_the_Shepherds_%281622%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Gerard_van_Honthorst_-_Adoration_of_the_Shepherds_%281622%29.jpg/440px-Gerard_van_Honthorst_-_Adoration_of_the_Shepherds_%281622%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gerard_van_Honthorst_-_Adoration_of_the_Shepherds_(1622).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Adoration of the Shepherds</i> by <a href="/wiki/Gerard_van_Honthorst" title="Gerard van Honthorst">Gerard van Honthorst</a>, 1622</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Matthew and Luke each describe Jesus' nativity (or birth), especially that Jesus was born by a virgin Mary in <a href="/wiki/Bethlehem" title="Bethlehem">Bethlehem</a> in fulfillment of prophecy. Luke's account emphasizes events before the <a href="/wiki/Nativity_of_Jesus" title="Nativity of Jesus">birth of Jesus</a> and centers on Mary, while Matthew's mostly covers those after the birth and centers on Joseph.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMillsBullard1998556_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMillsBullard1998556-122">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-marsh37_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-marsh37-123">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorris199226_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorris199226-124">[112]</a></sup> Both accounts state that Jesus was born to <a href="/wiki/Saint_Joseph" title="Saint Joseph">Joseph</a> and Mary, his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Betrothed" title="Betrothed">betrothed</a>, in Bethlehem, and both support the doctrine of the <a href="/wiki/Virgin_birth_of_Jesus" title="Virgin birth of Jesus">virgin birth of Jesus</a>, according to which Jesus was miraculously conceived by the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Spirit" title="Holy Spirit">Holy Spirit</a> in Mary's womb when she was still a virgin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jeffrey_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jeffrey-125">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoxEasley200730.E2.80.9337_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoxEasley200730.E2.80.9337-126">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[115]</a></sup></p>
<p>In Matthew, Joseph is troubled because Mary, his betrothed, is pregnant (<span class="nourlexpansion nowrap"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Matthew+1%3A19%E2%80%9320&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">Matthew 1:19–20</a></span>), but in the first of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St._Joseph%27s_dream" title="St. Joseph's dream">Joseph's three dreams</a> an angel assures him not to be afraid to take Mary as his wife, because her child was conceived by the Holy Spirit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Talbert_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-Talbert-128">[116]</a></sup> In <span class="nourlexpansion nowrap"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Matthew+2%3A1%E2%80%9312&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">Matthew 2:1–12</a></span>, <a href="/wiki/Biblical_Magi" title="Biblical Magi">wise men</a> or <a href="/wiki/Magi" title="Magi">Magi</a> from the East bring gifts to the young Jesus as the <a href="/wiki/Jesus,_King_of_the_Jews" title="Jesus, King of the Jews">King of the Jews</a>. <a href="/wiki/Herod_the_Great" title="Herod the Great">Herod the Great</a> hears of Jesus' birth and, wanting him killed, orders the murders of male infants in Bethlehem. But an angel warns Joseph in his second dream, and the family <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flight_to_Egypt" title="Flight to Egypt">flees to Egypt</a>—later to return and settle in <a href="/wiki/Nazareth" title="Nazareth">Nazareth</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Talbert_128-1"><a href="#cite_note-Talbert-128">[116]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarris1985272.E2.80.9385_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarris1985272.E2.80.9385-129">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[118]</a></sup></p>
<p>In <span class="nourlexpansion nowrap"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Luke+1%3A31%E2%80%9338&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">Luke 1:31–38</a></span>, Mary learns from the angel <a href="/wiki/Gabriel" title="Gabriel">Gabriel</a> that she will conceive and bear a child called Jesus through the action of the Holy Spirit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-marsh37_123-1"><a href="#cite_note-marsh37-123">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jeffrey_125-1"><a href="#cite_note-Jeffrey-125">[113]</a></sup> When Mary is due to give birth, she and Joseph travel from Nazareth to Joseph's ancestral home in Bethlehem to register in the census ordered by <a href="/wiki/Augustus" title="Augustus">Caesar Augustus</a>. While there Mary gives birth to Jesus, and as they have found no room in the inn, she places the newborn in a <a href="/wiki/Manger" title="Manger">manger</a> (<span class="nourlexpansion nowrap"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Luke+2%3A1%E2%80%937&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">Luke 2:1–7</a></span>). An <a href="/wiki/Annunciation_to_the_shepherds" title="Annunciation to the shepherds">angel announces the birth to some shepherds</a>, who go to Bethlehem to see Jesus, and subsequently spread the news abroad (<span class="nourlexpansion nowrap"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Luke+2%3A8-20&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">Luke 2:8-20</a></span>). After the <a href="/wiki/Presentation_of_Jesus_at_the_Temple" title="Presentation of Jesus at the Temple">presentation of Jesus at the Temple</a>, Joseph, Mary and Jesus return to Nazareth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-marsh37_123-2"><a href="#cite_note-marsh37-123">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jeffrey_125-2"><a href="#cite_note-Jeffrey-125">[113]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="Early_life,_family,_and_profession"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life.2C_family.2C_and_profession">Early life, family, and profession</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Christ_Child" title="Christ Child">Christ Child</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Return_of_the_family_of_Jesus_to_Nazareth" title="Return of the family of Jesus to Nazareth">Return of the family of Jesus to Nazareth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Unknown_years_of_Jesus" title="Unknown years of Jesus">Unknown years of Jesus</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Brothers_of_Jesus" title="Brothers of Jesus">Brothers of Jesus</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:232px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ChristInTheTemple.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="520" data-file-width="700" height="171" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/ChristInTheTemple.jpg/230px-ChristInTheTemple.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/ChristInTheTemple.jpg/345px-ChristInTheTemple.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/ChristInTheTemple.jpg/460px-ChristInTheTemple.jpg 2x" width="230"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ChristInTheTemple.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
12-year-old Jesus <a href="/wiki/Finding_in_the_Temple" title="Finding in the Temple">in the temple</a>, depicted by Heinrich Hofmann, 1884</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Jesus' childhood home is identified in the gospels of Luke and Matthew as the town of Nazareth in <a href="/wiki/Galilee" title="Galilee">Galilee</a>, where he lived with his family. Although Joseph appears in descriptions of Jesus' childhood, no mention is made of him thereafter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[119]</a></sup> His other family members—his mother, Mary, <a href="/wiki/Brothers_of_Jesus" title="Brothers of Jesus">his brothers</a> <a href="/wiki/James,_brother_of_Jesus" title="James, brother of Jesus">James</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Joses,_brother_of_Jesus" title="Joses, brother of Jesus">Joses (or Joseph)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jude,_brother_of_Jesus" title="Jude, brother of Jesus">Judas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Simon,_brother_of_Jesus" title="Simon, brother of Jesus">Simon</a> and his unnamed sisters—are mentioned in the gospels and other sources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aslan2013_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-Aslan2013-132">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[121]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Gospel of Mark reports that Jesus comes into conflict with his neighbors and family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarris1985270.E2.80.9372_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarris1985270.E2.80.9372-134">[122]</a></sup> Jesus' mother and brothers come to get him (<span class="nourlexpansion nowrap"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Mark+3%3A31%E2%80%9335&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">Mark 3:31–35</a></span>) because people are saying that he is crazy (<span class="nourlexpansion nowrap"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Mark+3%3A21&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">Mark 3:21</a></span>). Jesus responds that his followers are his true family. In John, Mary follows Jesus to his crucifixion, and he expresses concern over her well-being (<span class="nourlexpansion nowrap"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=John+19%3A25%E2%80%9327&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">John 19:25–27</a></span>).</p>
<p>Jesus is called a τέκτων (<i><a href="/wiki/Tekt%C5%8Dn" title="Tektōn">tektōn</a></i>) in <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(American_Standard)/Mark#6:3" title="s:Bible (American Standard)/Mark">Mark 6:3</a>, traditionally understood as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carpenter" title="Carpenter">carpenter</a> but could cover makers of objects in various materials, including builders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDickson200868.E2.80.9369_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDickson200868.E2.80.9369-136">[124]</a></sup> The gospels indicate that Jesus could read, paraphrase, and debate scripture, but this does not necessarily mean that he received formal scribal training.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-literacy_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-literacy-137">[125]</a></sup></p>
<p>When Jesus is presented in the temple per Jewish Law, a man named <a href="/wiki/Simeon_(Gospel_of_Luke)" title="Simeon (Gospel of Luke)">Simeon</a> says to Mary and Joseph that Jesus "shall stand as a sign of contradiction, while a sword will pierce your own soul. Then the secret thoughts of many will come to light" (<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(American_Standard)/Luke#2:35" title="s:Bible (American Standard)/Luke">Luke 2:28–35</a>). When Jesus goes missing, they find him in the temple sitting among the teachers, listening to them and asking questions, and the people are amazed at his understanding and answers; Mary scolds Jesus for going missing, to which Jesus replies that he must "be in his father's house" (<span class="nourlexpansion nowrap"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Luke+2%3A41%E2%80%9352&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">Luke 2:41–52</a></span>).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Baptism_and_temptation">Baptism and temptation</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Baptism_of_Jesus" title="Baptism of Jesus">Baptism of Jesus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Temptation_of_Christ" title="Temptation of Christ">Temptation of Christ</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Trevisani_baptism_christ.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="305" height="289" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Trevisani_baptism_christ.JPG/220px-Trevisani_baptism_christ.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a7/Trevisani_baptism_christ.JPG 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Trevisani_baptism_christ.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Trevisani" title="Trevisani">Trevisani</a>'s depiction of the <a href="/wiki/Baptism_of_Jesus" title="Baptism of Jesus">baptism of Jesus</a>, with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Holy_Spirit_(Christianity)" title="Holy Spirit (Christianity)">Holy Spirit</a> descending from Heaven as a dove</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Synoptic accounts of Jesus' baptism are all preceded by information about <a href="/wiki/John_the_Baptist" title="John the Baptist">John the Baptist</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlomberg2009224.E2.80.9329_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlomberg2009224.E2.80.9329-138">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEK.C3.B6stenbergerKellumQuarles2009141.E2.80.9343_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEK.C3.B6stenbergerKellumQuarles2009141.E2.80.9343-139">[127]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcGrath200616.E2.80.9322_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcGrath200616.E2.80.9322-140">[128]</a></sup> They show John preaching penance and repentance for the remission of sins and encouraging the giving of <a href="/wiki/Alms" title="Alms">alms</a> to the poor (<span class="nourlexpansion nowrap"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Luke+3%3A11&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">Luke 3:11</a></span>) as he baptizes people in the area of the <a href="/wiki/Jordan_River" title="Jordan River">Jordan River</a> around <a href="/wiki/Perea" title="Perea">Perea</a> and foretells (<span class="nourlexpansion nowrap"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Luke+3%3A16&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">Luke 3:16</a></span>) the arrival of someone "more powerful" than he.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EerRoger_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-EerRoger-141">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zanzig_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zanzig-142">[130]</a></sup> Later, Jesus identifies John as "the Elijah who was to come" (<span class="nourlexpansion nowrap"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Matthew+11%3A14&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">Matthew 11:14</a></span>, <span class="nourlexpansion nowrap"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Mark+9%3A13%E2%80%9314&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">Mark 9:13–14</a></span>), the prophet who was expected to arrive before the "great and terrible day of the Lord" (<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(American_Standard)/Malachi#4:5" title="s:Bible (American Standard)/Malachi">Malachi 4:5</a>). Likewise, Luke says that John had the spirit and power of <a href="/wiki/Elijah" title="Elijah">Elijah</a> (<span class="nourlexpansion nowrap"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Luke+1%3A17&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">Luke 1:17</a></span>).</p>
<p>In Mark, John baptizes Jesus, and as he comes out of the water he sees the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Spirit" title="Holy Spirit">Holy Spirit</a> descending to him like a dove and he hears a voice from heaven declaring him to be God's Son (<span class="nourlexpansion nowrap"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Mark+1%3A9-11&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">Mark 1:9-11</a></span>). This is one of two events described in the gospels where a voice from Heaven calls Jesus "Son", the other being the Transfiguration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELee200421.E2.80.9330_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELee200421.E2.80.9330-143">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nobbs_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nobbs-144">[132]</a></sup> The spirit then drives him into the wilderness where he is tempted by <a href="/wiki/Satan" title="Satan">Satan</a> (<span class="nourlexpansion nowrap"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Mark+1%3A12%E2%80%9313&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">Mark 1:12–13</a></span>). Jesus then begins his ministry after John's arrest (<span class="nourlexpansion nowrap"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Mark+1%3A14&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">Mark 1:14</a></span>). Jesus' baptism in Matthew is similar. Here, before Jesus' baptism, John protests, saying, "I need to be baptized by you" (<span class="nourlexpansion nowrap"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Matthew+3%3A14&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">Matthew 3:14</a></span>). Jesus instructs him to carry on with the baptism "to fulfill all righteousness" (<span class="nourlexpansion nowrap"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Matthew+3%3A15&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">Matthew 3:15</a></span>). Matthew also details the three temptations that Satan offers Jesus in the wilderness (<span class="nourlexpansion nowrap"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Matthew+4%3A3%E2%80%9311&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">Matthew 4:3–11</a></span>). In Luke, the Holy Spirit descends as a dove after everyone has been baptized and Jesus is praying (<span class="nourlexpansion nowrap"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Luke+3%3A21-22&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">Luke 3:21-22</a></span>). John implicitly recognizes Jesus from prison after sending his followers to ask about him <span class="nourlexpansion nowrap"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Luke+7%3A18%E2%80%9323&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">Luke 7:18–23</a></span>). Jesus' baptism and temptation serve as preparation for his public ministry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[133]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Gospel of John leaves out Jesus' baptism and temptation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrossLivingstone2005Jesus_Christ_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrossLivingstone2005Jesus_Christ-146">[134]</a></sup> Here, John the Baptist testifies that he saw the Spirit descend on Jesus <span class="nourlexpansion nowrap"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=John+1%3A32&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">John 1:32</a></span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zanzig_142-1"><a href="#cite_note-Zanzig-142">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoringCraddock2004.5Bhttps:.2F.2Fbooks.google.com.2Fbooks.3Fid.3DN0tLXRIiIe0C.26lpg.3DPA292.26pg.3DPA292.23v.3Donepage.26q.3D.2522the.2520baptist.2522.26f.3Dfalse_292.5D_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoringCraddock2004.5Bhttps:.2F.2Fbooks.google.com.2Fbooks.3Fid.3DN0tLXRIiIe0C.26lpg.3DPA292.26pg.3DPA292.23v.3Donepage.26q.3D.2522the.2520baptist.2522.26f.3Dfalse_292.5D-147">[135]</a></sup> John publicly proclaims Jesus as the sacrificial <a href="/wiki/Lamb_of_God" title="Lamb of God">Lamb of God</a>, and some of John's followers become disciples of Jesus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETheissenMerz199817.E2.80.9362_101-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETheissenMerz199817.E2.80.9362-101">[89]</a></sup> In this Gospel, John denies that he is Elijah <span class="nourlexpansion nowrap"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=John+1%3A21&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">John 1:21</a></span>). Before John is imprisoned, Jesus leads his followers to baptize disciples as well (<span class="nourlexpansion nowrap"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=John+3%3A22%E2%80%9324&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">John 3:22–24</a></span>), and they baptize more people than John (<span class="nourlexpansion nowrap"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=John+4%3A1&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">John 4:1</a></span>).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Public_ministry"><span id="Ministry"></span> Public ministry</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Jesus" title="Ministry of Jesus">Ministry of Jesus</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bloch-SermonOnTheMount.jpg"><img alt="Jesus sits atop a mount, preaching to a crowd" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1545" data-file-width="1377" height="247" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Bloch-SermonOnTheMount.jpg/220px-Bloch-SermonOnTheMount.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Bloch-SermonOnTheMount.jpg/330px-Bloch-SermonOnTheMount.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Bloch-SermonOnTheMount.jpg/440px-Bloch-SermonOnTheMount.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bloch-SermonOnTheMount.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A 19th century painting depicting the <a href="/wiki/Sermon_on_the_Mount" title="Sermon on the Mount">Sermon on the Mount</a>, by <a href="/wiki/Carl_Bloch" title="Carl Bloch">Carl Bloch</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Synoptics depict two distinct geographical settings in Jesus' ministry. The first takes place north of <a href="/wiki/Judea" title="Judea">Judea</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Galilee" title="Galilee">Galilee</a>, where Jesus conducts a successful ministry; and the second shows Jesus rejected and killed when he travels to <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem" title="Jerusalem">Jerusalem</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ISBEO_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-ISBEO-34">[25]</a></sup> Often referred to as "<a href="/wiki/Rabbi" title="Rabbi">rabbi</a>",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ISBEO_34-2"><a href="#cite_note-ISBEO-34">[25]</a></sup> Jesus preaches his message orally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2013_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dunn2013-33">[24]</a></sup> Notably, Jesus forbids those who recognize him as the Messiah to speak of it, including people he heals and demons he exorcises (see <a href="/wiki/Messianic_Secret" title="Messianic Secret">Messianic Secret</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarris1985285.E2.80.9396_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarris1985285.E2.80.9396-148">[136]</a></sup></p>
<p>John depicts Jesus' ministry as largely taking place in and around Jerusalem, rather than in Galilee; and Jesus' divine identity is openly proclaimed and immediately recognized.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarris1985302.E2.80.9310_115-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarris1985302.E2.80.9310-115">[103]</a></sup></p>
<p>Scholars divide the ministry of Jesus into several stages. The Galilean ministry begins when Jesus returns to Galilee from the <a href="/wiki/Judaean_Desert" title="Judaean Desert">Judaean Desert</a> after rebuffing the temptation of <a href="/wiki/Satan" title="Satan">Satan</a>. Jesus preaches around Galilee, and in <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(American_Standard)/Matthew#4:18" title="s:Bible (American Standard)/Matthew">Matthew 4:18–20</a>, <a href="/wiki/First_disciples_of_Jesus" title="First disciples of Jesus">his first disciples</a>, who will eventually form the core of the early Church, encounter him and begin to travel with him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcGrath200616.E2.80.9322_140-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcGrath200616.E2.80.9322-140">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERedford2007117.E2.80.9330_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERedford2007117.E2.80.9330-149">[137]</a></sup> This period includes the <a href="/wiki/Sermon_on_the_Mount" title="Sermon on the Mount">Sermon on the Mount</a>, one of Jesus' major discourses,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERedford2007117.E2.80.9330_149-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERedford2007117.E2.80.9330-149">[137]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[138]</a></sup> as well as the <a href="/wiki/Calming_the_storm" title="Calming the storm">calming of the storm</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Feeding_the_multitude" title="Feeding the multitude">feeding of the 5,000</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jesus_walking_on_water" title="Jesus walking on water">walking on water</a> and a number of other miracles and <a href="/wiki/Parables_of_Jesus" title="Parables of Jesus">parables</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERedford2007143.E2.80.9360_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERedford2007143.E2.80.9360-151">[139]</a></sup> It ends with the <a href="/wiki/Confession_of_Peter" title="Confession of Peter">Confession of Peter</a> and the Transfiguration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[140]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barton132_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-Barton132-153">[141]</a></sup></p>
<p>As Jesus travels towards Jerusalem, in the <a href="/wiki/Perea" title="Perea">Perean</a> ministry, he returns to the area where he was baptized, about a third of the way down from the <a href="/wiki/Sea_of_Galilee" title="Sea of Galilee">Sea of Galilee</a> along the <a href="/wiki/Jordan_River" title="Jordan River">Jordan River</a> (<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(American_Standard)/John#10:40" title="s:Bible (American Standard)/John">John 10:40–42</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoxEasley2007137_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoxEasley2007137-154">[142]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERedford2007211.E2.80.9329_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERedford2007211.E2.80.9329-155">[143]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Jesus#Final_ministry_in_Jerusalem" title="Ministry of Jesus">final ministry in Jerusalem</a> begins with Jesus' <a href="/wiki/Triumphal_entry_into_Jerusalem" title="Triumphal entry into Jerusalem">triumphal entry</a> into the city on <a href="/wiki/Palm_Sunday" title="Palm Sunday">Palm Sunday</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoxEasley2007155.E2.80.9370_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoxEasley2007155.E2.80.9370-156">[144]</a></sup> In the Synoptic Gospels, during that week Jesus <a href="/wiki/Cleansing_of_the_Temple" title="Cleansing of the Temple">drives the money changers</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Second_Temple" title="Second Temple">Second Temple</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bargain_of_Judas" title="Bargain of Judas">Judas bargains to betray</a> him. This period culminates in the <a href="/wiki/Last_Supper" title="Last Supper">Last Supper</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Farewell_Discourse" title="Farewell Discourse">Farewell Discourse</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlomberg2009224.E2.80.9329_138-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlomberg2009224.E2.80.9329-138">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoxEasley2007155.E2.80.9370_156-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoxEasley2007155.E2.80.9370-156">[144]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERedford2007257.E2.80.9374_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERedford2007257.E2.80.9374-157">[145]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Disciples_and_followers">Disciples and followers</span></h4>
<p>Near the beginning of his ministry, Jesus <a href="/wiki/Commissioning_of_the_Twelve_Apostles" title="Commissioning of the Twelve Apostles">appoints twelve apostles</a>. In Matthew and Mark, despite Jesus only briefly requesting that they join him, Jesus' first four apostles, who were fishermen, are described as immediately consenting, and abandoning their nets and boats to do so (<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(American_Standard)/Matthew#4:18" title="s:Bible (American Standard)/Matthew">Matthew 4:18–22</a>, <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(American_Standard)/Mark#1:16" title="s:Bible (American Standard)/Mark">Mark 1:16–20</a>). In John, Jesus' first two apostles were disciples of John the Baptist. The Baptist sees Jesus and calls him the <a href="/wiki/Lamb_of_God" title="Lamb of God">Lamb of God</a>; the two hear this and follow Jesus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrown198825.E2.80.9327_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrown198825.E2.80.9327-158">[146]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoringCraddock2004292.E2.80.9393_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoringCraddock2004292.E2.80.9393-159">[147]</a></sup> In addition to the Twelve Apostles, the opening of the passage of the <a href="/wiki/Sermon_on_the_Plain" title="Sermon on the Plain">Sermon on the Plain</a> identifies a much larger group of people as disciples (<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(American_Standard)/Luke#6:17" title="s:Bible (American Standard)/Luke">Luke 6:17</a>). Also, in <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(American_Standard)/Luke#10:1" title="s:Bible (American Standard)/Luke">Luke 10:1–16</a> Jesus sends <a href="/wiki/Seventy_disciples" title="Seventy disciples">seventy or seventy-two of his followers</a> in pairs to prepare towns for his prospective visit. They are instructed to accept hospitality, heal the sick and spread the word that the <a href="/wiki/Kingship_and_kingdom_of_God" title="Kingship and kingdom of God">Kingdom of God</a> is coming.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[148]</a></sup></p>
<p>In Mark, the disciples are notably obtuse. They fail to understand Jesus' miracles (<span class="nourlexpansion nowrap"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Mark+4%3A35%E2%80%9341&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">Mark 4:35–41</a></span>, <span class="nourlexpansion nowrap"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Mark+6%3A52&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">Mark 6:52</a></span>), his parables (<span class="nourlexpansion nowrap"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Mark+4%3A13&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">Mark 4:13</a></span>), or what "rising from the dead" would mean (<span class="nourlexpansion nowrap"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Mark+9%3A9%E2%80%9310&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">Mark 9:9–10</a></span>). When Jesus is later arrested, they desert him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarris1985285.E2.80.9396_148-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarris1985285.E2.80.9396-148">[136]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Teachings_and_miracles"><span id="Teachings_and_preachings"></span> Teachings and miracles</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Sermon_on_the_Mount" title="Sermon on the Mount">Sermon on the Mount</a>, <a href="/wiki/Parables_of_Jesus" title="Parables of Jesus">Parables of Jesus</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Miracles_of_Jesus" title="Miracles of Jesus">Miracles of Jesus</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Sermon_on_the_Plain" title="Sermon on the Plain">Sermon on the Plain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Five_Discourses_of_Matthew" title="Five Discourses of Matthew">Five Discourses of Matthew</a>, <a href="/wiki/Farewell_Discourse" title="Farewell Discourse">Farewell Discourse</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Olivet_Discourse" title="Olivet Discourse">Olivet Discourse</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hoffman-ChristAndTheRichYoungRuler.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3412" data-file-width="4324" height="174" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Hoffman-ChristAndTheRichYoungRuler.jpg/220px-Hoffman-ChristAndTheRichYoungRuler.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Hoffman-ChristAndTheRichYoungRuler.jpg/330px-Hoffman-ChristAndTheRichYoungRuler.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Hoffman-ChristAndTheRichYoungRuler.jpg/440px-Hoffman-ChristAndTheRichYoungRuler.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hoffman-ChristAndTheRichYoungRuler.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i><a href="/wiki/Jesus_and_the_rich_young_man" title="Jesus and the rich young man">Christ and the Rich Young Ruler</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Hofmann_(painter)" title="Heinrich Hofmann (painter)">Heinrich Hofmann</a>, 1889</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the Synoptics, Jesus teaches extensively, often in <a href="/wiki/Parable" title="Parable">parables</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETheissenMerz1998316.E2.80.9346_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETheissenMerz1998316.E2.80.9346-161">[149]</a></sup> about the Kingdom of God (or, in Matthew, the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Heaven_(Gospel_of_Matthew)" title="Kingdom of Heaven (Gospel of Matthew)">Kingdom of Heaven</a>). The Kingdom is described as both imminent (<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(American_Standard)/Mark#1:15" title="s:Bible (American Standard)/Mark">Mark 1:15</a>) and already present in the ministry of Jesus (<span class="nourlexpansion nowrap"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Luke+17%3A21&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">Luke 17:21</a></span>). Jesus promises inclusion in the Kingdom for those who accept his message (<span class="nourlexpansion nowrap"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Mark+10%3A13%E2%80%9327&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">Mark 10:13–27</a></span>). Jesus talks of the "<a href="/wiki/Son_of_man" title="Son of man">Son of Man</a>," an apocalyptic figure who would come to gather the chosen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_53-3"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-53">[43]</a></sup></p>
<p>Jesus calls people to repent their sins and to devote themselves completely to God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_53-4"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-53">[43]</a></sup> Jesus tells his followers to adhere to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jewish_law" title="Jewish law">Jewish law</a>, although he is perceived by some to have broken the law himself, for example regarding the <a href="/wiki/Sabbath" title="Sabbath">Sabbath</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_53-5"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-53">[43]</a></sup> When asked what the greatest commandment is, Jesus replies: "You shall love the Lord your God with all your heart, and with all your soul, and with all your mind ... And a second is like it: 'You shall love your neighbor as yourself" (<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(American_Standard)/Matthew#22:37" title="s:Bible (American Standard)/Matthew">Matthew 22:37–39</a>). Other ethical teachings of Jesus include <a href="/wiki/Matthew_5:44" title="Matthew 5:44">loving your enemies</a>, refraining from hatred and lust, <a href="/wiki/Turning_the_other_cheek" title="Turning the other cheek">turning the other cheek</a>, and forgiving people who have sinned against you (<a href="/wiki/Sermon_on_the_Mount" title="Sermon on the Mount">Matthew 5–7</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[150]</a></sup></p>
<p>John's Gospel presents the teachings of Jesus not merely as his own preaching, but as divine <a href="/wiki/Revelation" title="Revelation">revelation</a>. John the Baptist, for example, states in <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(American_Standard)/John#3:34" title="s:Bible (American Standard)/John">John 3:34</a>: "He whom God has sent speaks the words of God, for he gives the Spirit without measure." In <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(American_Standard)/John#7:16" title="s:Bible (American Standard)/John">John 7:16</a> Jesus says, "My teaching is not mine but his who sent me." He asserts the same thing in <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(American_Standard)/John#14:10" title="s:Bible (American Standard)/John">John 14:10</a>: "Do you not believe that I am in the Father and the Father is in me? The words that I say to you I do not speak on my own; but the Father who dwells in me does his works."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated98_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated98-163">[151]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[152]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Christ_cleans_leper_man.jpg"><img alt="Jesus, his head surrounded by a halo, puts his hands on a leper, thereby healing him" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="985" data-file-width="689" height="243" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Christ_cleans_leper_man.jpg/170px-Christ_cleans_leper_man.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Christ_cleans_leper_man.jpg/255px-Christ_cleans_leper_man.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Christ_cleans_leper_man.jpg/340px-Christ_cleans_leper_man.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Christ_cleans_leper_man.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Jesus_cleansing_a_leper" title="Jesus cleansing a leper">Jesus cleansing a leper</a>, medieval <a href="/wiki/Mosaic" title="Mosaic">mosaic</a> from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monreale_Cathedral" title="Monreale Cathedral">Monreale Cathedral</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Approximately thirty parables form about one third of Jesus' recorded teachings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated98_163-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated98-163">[151]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[153]</a></sup> The parables appear within longer sermons and at other places in the narrative.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[154]</a></sup> They often contain symbolism, and usually relate the physical world to the <a href="/wiki/Spirituality" title="Spirituality">spiritual</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[155]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[156]</a></sup> Common themes in these tales include the kindness and generosity of God and the perils of transgression.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[157]</a></sup> Some of his parables, such as the <a href="/wiki/Parable_of_the_Prodigal_Son" title="Parable of the Prodigal Son">Prodigal Son</a> (<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(American_Standard)/Luke#15:11" title="s:Bible (American Standard)/Luke">Luke 15:11–32</a>), are relatively simple, while others, such as the <a href="/wiki/Parable_of_the_Growing_Seed" title="Parable of the Growing Seed">Growing Seed</a> (<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(American_Standard)/Mark#4:26" title="s:Bible (American Standard)/Mark">Mark 4:26–29</a>), are sophisticated, profound and abstruse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[158]</a></sup> When asked by his disciples about why he speaks in parables to the people, Jesus replies that the chosen disciples have been given to "know the secrets of the kingdom of heaven", unlike the rest of their people, "For the one who has will be given more and he will have in abundance. But the one who does not have will be deprived even more.", going on to say that the majority of their generation have grown "dull hearts" and thus are unable to understand (<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(American_Standard)/Matthew#13:10" title="s:Bible (American Standard)/Matthew">Matthew 13:10–17</a>).</p>
<p>In the gospel accounts, Jesus devotes a large portion of his ministry performing <a href="/wiki/Miracle" title="Miracle">miracles</a>, especially healings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenMcKnightMarshall1992299_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenMcKnightMarshall1992299-171">[159]</a></sup> The miracles can be classified into two main categories: healing miracles and nature miracles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETwelftree1999350_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETwelftree1999350-172">[160]</a></sup> The healing miracles include cures for physical ailments, <a href="/wiki/Exorcism" title="Exorcism">exorcisms</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWitherington1997113_109-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWitherington1997113-109">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETheissenMerz1998298_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETheissenMerz1998298-173">[161]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Resurrection_of_the_dead" title="Resurrection of the dead">resurrections of the dead</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenMcKnightMarshall1992300_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenMcKnightMarshall1992300-174">[162]</a></sup> The nature miracles show Jesus' power over nature, and include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turning_water_into_wine" title="Turning water into wine">turning water into wine</a>, walking on water, and calming a storm, among others. Jesus states that his miracles are from a divine source. When Jesus' opponents suddenly accuse him of performing exorcisms by the power of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beelzebul" title="Beelzebul">Beelzebul</a>, the prince of demons, Jesus counters that he performs them by the "Spirit of God" (<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(American_Standard)/Matthew#12:28" title="s:Bible (American Standard)/Matthew">Matthew 12:28</a>) or "finger of God", arguing that all logic suggests that Satan would not let his demons assist the Children of God because it would divide Satan's house and bring his kingdom to desolation; furthermore, he asks his opponents that if he exorcises by <a href="/wiki/Beelzebub" title="Beelzebub">Beel'zebub</a>, "by whom do your sons cast them out?"(<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(American_Standard)/Luke#11:20" title="s:Bible (American Standard)/Luke">Luke 11:20</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica2_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica2-175">[163]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[164]</a></sup> In <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(American_Standard)/Matthew#12:28" title="s:Bible (American Standard)/Matthew">Matthew 12:31–32</a>, he goes on to say that while all manner of sin, "even insults against God" or "insults against the son of man", shall be forgiven, whoever insults goodness (or "The <a href="/wiki/Holy_Spirit" title="Holy Spirit">Holy Spirit</a>") shall never be forgiven; he/she carries the guilt of his/her sin forever.</p>
<p>In John, Jesus' miracles are described as "signs", performed to prove his mission and divinity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sign_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sign-177">[165]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[166]</a></sup> However, in the Synoptics, when asked by some teachers of the Law and some Pharisees to give miraculous signs to prove his authority, Jesus refuses,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sign_177-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sign-177">[165]</a></sup> saying that no sign shall come to corrupt and evil people except the sign of the prophet <a href="/wiki/Jonah" title="Jonah">Jonah</a>. Also, in the Synoptic Gospels, the crowds regularly respond to Jesus' miracles with awe and press on him to heal their sick.
