In John's Gospel, Jesus is presented as unpressured by the crowds, who often respond to his miracles with trust and faith.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETwelftree1999236_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETwelftree1999236-179">[167]</a></sup> One characteristic shared among all miracles of Jesus in the gospel accounts is that he performed them freely and never requested or accepted any form of payment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[168]</a></sup> The gospel episodes that include descriptions of the miracles of Jesus also often include teachings, and the miracles themselves involve an element of teaching.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WPent212_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-WPent212-181">[169]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETwelftree199995_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETwelftree199995-182">[170]</a></sup> Many of the miracles teach the importance of faith. In the <a href="/wiki/Cleansing_ten_lepers" title="Cleansing ten lepers">cleansing of ten lepers</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Daughter_of_Jairus" title="Daughter of Jairus">raising of Jairus' daughter</a>, for instance, the beneficiaries are told that their healing was due to their faith.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDonahueHarrington2002182_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDonahueHarrington2002182-183">[171]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[172]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Proclamation_as_Christ_and_Transfiguration">Proclamation as Christ and Transfiguration</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Confession_of_Peter" title="Confession of Peter">Confession of Peter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Transfiguration_of_Jesus" title="Transfiguration of Jesus">Transfiguration of Jesus</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Transfigurationbloch.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="912" data-file-width="734" height="273" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Transfigurationbloch.jpg/220px-Transfigurationbloch.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Transfigurationbloch.jpg/330px-Transfigurationbloch.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Transfigurationbloch.jpg/440px-Transfigurationbloch.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Transfigurationbloch.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Transfiguration_of_Jesus" title="Transfiguration of Jesus">Transfiguration of Jesus</a>, depicted by <a href="/wiki/Carl_Bloch" title="Carl Bloch">Carl Bloch</a>, 19th century</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>At about the middle of each of the three Synoptic Gospels are two significant events: the Confession of <a href="/wiki/Saint_Peter" title="Saint Peter">Peter</a> and the Transfiguration of Jesus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barton132_153-1"><a href="#cite_note-Barton132-153">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KingsburyMark_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-KingsburyMark-185">[173]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELee200421.E2.80.9330_143-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELee200421.E2.80.9330-143">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nobbs_144-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nobbs-144">[132]</a></sup> These two events are not mentioned in the Gospel of John.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrossLivingstone2005John.2C_Gospel_of_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrossLivingstone2005John.2C_Gospel_of-186">[174]</a></sup></p>
<p>In his Confession, <a href="/wiki/Saint_Peter" title="Saint Peter">Peter</a> tells Jesus, "You are the Messiah, the Son of the living God."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Karris_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-Karris-187">[175]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[176]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDonahueHarrington2002336_189-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDonahueHarrington2002336-189">[177]</a></sup> Jesus affirms that Peter's confession is divinely revealed truth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OneTeacher_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-OneTeacher-190">[178]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPannenberg196853.E2.80.9354_191-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPannenberg196853.E2.80.9354-191">[179]</a></sup> After the confession, Jesus tells his disciples about his upcoming death and resurrection (Matthew 16:21, Mark 8:31, Luke 9:22)</p>
<p>In the Transfiguration (<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(American_Standard)/Matthew#17:1" title="s:Bible (American Standard)/Matthew">Matthew 17:1–9</a>, <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(American_Standard)/Mark#9:2" title="s:Bible (American Standard)/Mark">Mark 9:2–8</a>, and <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(American_Standard)/Luke#9:28" title="s:Bible (American Standard)/Luke">Luke 9:28–36</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELee200421.E2.80.9330_143-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELee200421.E2.80.9330-143">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nobbs_144-2"><a href="#cite_note-Nobbs-144">[132]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barton132_153-2"><a href="#cite_note-Barton132-153">[141]</a></sup> Jesus takes Peter and two other apostles up an unnamed mountain, where "he was transfigured before them, and his face shone like the sun, and his clothes became dazzling white."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELee200472.E2.80.9376_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELee200472.E2.80.9376-192">[180]</a></sup> A bright cloud appears around them, and a voice from the cloud says, "This is my Son, the Beloved; with him I am well pleased; listen to him" (<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(American_Standard)/Matthew#17:1" title="s:Bible (American Standard)/Matthew">Matthew 17:1–9</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELee200421.E2.80.9330_143-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELee200421.E2.80.9330-143">[131]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Passion_Week">Passion Week</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Passion_Week" title="Passion Week">Passion Week</a></div>
<p>The description of the last week of the life of Jesus (often called <a href="/wiki/Passion_Week" title="Passion Week">Passion Week</a>) occupies about one third of the narrative in the canonical gospels,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Turner613_119-1"><a href="#cite_note-Turner613-119">[107]</a></sup> starting with Jesus' <a href="/wiki/Triumphal_entry_into_Jerusalem" title="Triumphal entry into Jerusalem">triumphal entry into Jerusalem</a> and ending with his Crucifixion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlomberg2009224.E2.80.9329_138-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlomberg2009224.E2.80.9329-138">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoxEasley2007155.E2.80.9370_156-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoxEasley2007155.E2.80.9370-156">[144]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Activities_in_Jerusalem">Activities in Jerusalem</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:G%C3%A9r%C3%B4me_-_L%27entr%C3%A9e_du_Christ_%C3%A0_J%C3%A9rusalem_-_cadre.jpg"><img alt="Jesus, riding a donkey colt, rides towards Jerusalem. A large crowd greets him outside the walls." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1422" data-file-width="2191" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/G%C3%A9r%C3%B4me_-_L%27entr%C3%A9e_du_Christ_%C3%A0_J%C3%A9rusalem_-_cadre.jpg/260px-G%C3%A9r%C3%B4me_-_L%27entr%C3%A9e_du_Christ_%C3%A0_J%C3%A9rusalem_-_cadre.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/G%C3%A9r%C3%B4me_-_L%27entr%C3%A9e_du_Christ_%C3%A0_J%C3%A9rusalem_-_cadre.jpg/390px-G%C3%A9r%C3%B4me_-_L%27entr%C3%A9e_du_Christ_%C3%A0_J%C3%A9rusalem_-_cadre.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/G%C3%A9r%C3%B4me_-_L%27entr%C3%A9e_du_Christ_%C3%A0_J%C3%A9rusalem_-_cadre.jpg/520px-G%C3%A9r%C3%B4me_-_L%27entr%C3%A9e_du_Christ_%C3%A0_J%C3%A9rusalem_-_cadre.jpg 2x" width="260"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:G%C3%A9r%C3%B4me_-_L%27entr%C3%A9e_du_Christ_%C3%A0_J%C3%A9rusalem_-_cadre.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A painting of Jesus' <a href="/wiki/Triumphal_entry_into_Jerusalem" title="Triumphal entry into Jerusalem">final entry into Jerusalem</a>, by <a href="/wiki/Jean-L%C3%A9on_G%C3%A9r%C3%B4me" title="Jean-Léon Gérôme">Jean-Léon Gérôme</a>, 1897</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Triumphal_entry_into_Jerusalem" title="Triumphal entry into Jerusalem">Triumphal entry into Jerusalem</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cleansing_of_the_Temple" title="Cleansing of the Temple">Cleansing of the Temple</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bargain_of_Judas" title="Bargain of Judas">Bargain of Judas</a></div>
<p>In the Synoptics, the last week in Jerusalem is the conclusion of the journey through Perea and <a href="/wiki/Judea" title="Judea">Judea</a> that Jesus began in Galilee.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoxEasley2007155.E2.80.9370_156-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoxEasley2007155.E2.80.9370-156">[144]</a></sup> Jesus rides a young donkey into Jerusalem, reflecting the tale of <a href="/wiki/The_Messiah%27s_Donkey" title="The Messiah's Donkey">the Messiah's Donkey</a>, an oracle from the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Zechariah" title="Book of Zechariah">Book of Zechariah</a> in which the Jews' humble king enters Jerusalem this way (<a class="external text" href="http://www.mechon-mamre.org/p/pt/pt2309.htm#9" rel="nofollow">Zechariah 9:9</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-May_Metzger_Mark_87-3"><a href="#cite_note-May_Metzger_Mark-87">[75]</a></sup> People along the way lay cloaks and small branches of trees (known as palm fronds) in front of him and sing part of <a class="external text" href="http://www.mechon-mamre.org/p/pt/pt26b8.htm#25" rel="nofollow">Psalms 118:25–26</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoringCraddock2004256.E2.80.9358_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoringCraddock2004256.E2.80.9358-193">[181]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMajern.C3.ADkPonessaManhardt2005133.E2.80.9334_194-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMajern.C3.ADkPonessaManhardt2005133.E2.80.9334-194">[182]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans2003381.E2.80.9395_195-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans2003381.E2.80.9395-195">[183]</a></sup></p>
<p>Jesus next expels the money changers from the <a href="/wiki/Second_Temple" title="Second Temple">Second Temple</a>, accusing them of turning it into a den of thieves through their commercial activities. Jesus then prophesies about the coming destruction, including false prophets, wars, earthquakes, celestial disorders, persecution of the faithful, the appearance of an "abomination of desolation," and unendurable tribulations (<span class="nourlexpansion nowrap"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Mark+13%3A1%E2%80%9323&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">Mark 13:1–23</a></span>). The mysterious "Son of Man," he says, will dispatch angels to gather the faithful from all parts of the earth (<span class="nourlexpansion nowrap"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Mark+13%3A24%E2%80%9327&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">Mark 13:24–27</a></span>). Jesus warns that these wonders will occur in the lifetimes of the hearers (<span class="nourlexpansion nowrap"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Mark+13%3A28-32&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">Mark 13:28-32</a></span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarris1985285.E2.80.9396_148-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarris1985285.E2.80.9396-148">[136]</a></sup> In John, the Cleansing of the Temple occurs at the beginning of Jesus' ministry instead of at the end (<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(American_Standard)/John#2:13" title="s:Bible (American Standard)/John">John 2:13–16</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarris1985302.E2.80.9310_115-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarris1985302.E2.80.9310-115">[103]</a></sup></p>
<p>Jesus comes into conflict with the Jewish elders, such as when they <a href="/wiki/Authority_of_Jesus_questioned" title="Authority of Jesus questioned">question his authority</a> and when he <a href="/wiki/Woes_of_the_Pharisees" title="Woes of the Pharisees">criticizes them and calls them hypocrites</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoringCraddock2004256.E2.80.9358_193-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoringCraddock2004256.E2.80.9358-193">[181]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans2003381.E2.80.9395_195-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans2003381.E2.80.9395-195">[183]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Judas_Iscariot" title="Judas Iscariot">Judas Iscariot</a>, one of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Twelve_apostles" title="Twelve apostles">twelve apostles</a>, secretly strikes a bargain with the Jewish elders, agreeing to betray Jesus to them for <a href="/wiki/Thirty_pieces_of_silver" title="Thirty pieces of silver">30 silver coins</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lockyer106_196-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lockyer106-196">[184]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[185]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Gospel of John recounts of two other feasts in which Jesus taught in Jerusalem before the Passion Week (<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(American_Standard)/John#7:1" title="s:Bible (American Standard)/John">John 7:1–10:42</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarris1985270.E2.80.9372_134-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarris1985270.E2.80.9372-134">[122]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Bethany_(biblical_village)" title="Bethany (biblical village)">Bethany</a>, a village near Jerusalem, Jesus <a href="/wiki/Raising_of_Lazarus" title="Raising of Lazarus">raises Lazarus from the dead</a>. This potent sign<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarris1985302.E2.80.9310_115-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarris1985302.E2.80.9310-115">[103]</a></sup> increases the tension with authorities,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoxEasley2007155.E2.80.9370_156-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoxEasley2007155.E2.80.9370-156">[144]</a></sup> who conspire to kill him (<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(American_Standard)/John#11:1" title="s:Bible (American Standard)/John">John 11</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarris1985270.E2.80.9372_134-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarris1985270.E2.80.9372-134">[122]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mary_of_Bethany" title="Mary of Bethany">Mary of Bethany</a> anoints Jesus' feet, foreshadowing his entombment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFunkHoover1993401.E2.80.9370_198-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFunkHoover1993401.E2.80.9370-198">[186]</a></sup> Jesus then makes his Messianic entry into Jerusalem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarris1985270.E2.80.9372_134-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarris1985270.E2.80.9372-134">[122]</a></sup> The cheering crowds greeting Jesus as he enters Jerusalem add to the animosity between him and the establishment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoxEasley2007155.E2.80.9370_156-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoxEasley2007155.E2.80.9370-156">[144]</a></sup> In John, Jesus has already cleansed the Second Temple during an earlier Passover visit to Jerusalem. John next recounts Jesus' Last Supper with his disciples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarris1985270.E2.80.9372_134-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarris1985270.E2.80.9372-134">[122]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Last_Supper">Last Supper</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Last_Supper" title="Last Supper">Last Supper</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Jesus_predicts_his_betrayal" title="Jesus predicts his betrayal">Jesus predicts his betrayal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Denial_of_Peter" title="Denial of Peter">Denial of Peter</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Last_Supper_in_Christian_art" title="Last Supper in Christian art">Last Supper in Christian art</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%C3%9Altima_Cena_-_Juan_de_Juanes.jpg"><img alt="A depiction of the Last Supper. Jesus sits in the center, his apostles gathered around on either side of him." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1922" data-file-width="3051" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/%C3%9Altima_Cena_-_Juan_de_Juanes.jpg/260px-%C3%9Altima_Cena_-_Juan_de_Juanes.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/%C3%9Altima_Cena_-_Juan_de_Juanes.jpg/390px-%C3%9Altima_Cena_-_Juan_de_Juanes.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/%C3%9Altima_Cena_-_Juan_de_Juanes.jpg/520px-%C3%9Altima_Cena_-_Juan_de_Juanes.jpg 2x" width="260"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%C3%9Altima_Cena_-_Juan_de_Juanes.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Last_Supper" title="Last Supper">Last Supper</a>, depicted by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Juan_de_Juanes" title="Juan de Juanes">Juan de Juanes</a>, c. 1562</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Last Supper is the final meal that Jesus shares with his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Twelve_Apostles#The_Twelve_Apostles" title="Twelve Apostles">12 apostles</a> in Jerusalem before his crucifixion. The Last Supper is mentioned in all four canonical gospels; Paul's <a href="/wiki/First_Epistle_to_the_Corinthians" title="First Epistle to the Corinthians">First Epistle to the Corinthians</a> (<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(American_Standard)/1_Corinthians#11:23" title="s:Bible (American Standard)/1 Corinthians">11:23–26</a>) also refers to it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fahlbusch52_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-Fahlbusch52-76">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans2003465.E2.80.9377_199-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans2003465.E2.80.9377-199">[187]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoxEasley2007180.E2.80.9391_200-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoxEasley2007180.E2.80.9391-200">[188]</a></sup> During the meal, <a href="/wiki/Jesus_predicts_his_betrayal" title="Jesus predicts his betrayal">Jesus predicts</a> that one of his apostles will betray him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoxEasley2007182_201-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoxEasley2007182-201">[189]</a></sup> Despite each Apostle's assertion that he would not betray him, Jesus reiterates that the betrayer would be one of those present. <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(American_Standard)/Matthew#26:23" title="s:Bible (American Standard)/Matthew">Matthew 26:23–25</a> and <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(American_Standard)/John#13:26" title="s:Bible (American Standard)/John">John 13:26–27</a> specifically identify Judas as the traitor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fahlbusch52_76-2"><a href="#cite_note-Fahlbusch52-76">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans2003465.E2.80.9377_199-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans2003465.E2.80.9377-199">[187]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoxEasley2007182_201-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoxEasley2007182-201">[189]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the Synoptics, Jesus takes bread, breaks it, and gives it to the disciples, saying, "This is my body, which is given for you". He then has them all drink from a cup, saying, "This cup that is poured out for you is the new covenant in my blood" (<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(American_Standard)/Luke#22:19" title="s:Bible (American Standard)/Luke">Luke 22:19–20</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fahlbusch52_76-3"><a href="#cite_note-Fahlbusch52-76">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrossLivingstone2005Eucharist_202-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrossLivingstone2005Eucharist-202">[190]</a></sup> The Christian <a href="/wiki/Sacrament" title="Sacrament">sacrament</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ordinance_(Christian)" title="Ordinance (Christian)">ordinance</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Eucharist" title="Eucharist">Eucharist</a> is based on these events.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[191]</a></sup> Although the Gospel of John does not include a description of the bread-and-wine ritual during the Last Supper, most scholars agree that <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(American_Standard)/John#6:22" title="s:Bible (American Standard)/John">John 6:22–59</a> (the <a href="/wiki/Bread_of_Life_Discourse" title="Bread of Life Discourse">Bread of Life Discourse</a>) has a eucharistic character and resonates with the <a href="/wiki/Origin_of_the_Eucharist#Institution_narratives" title="Origin of the Eucharist">institution narratives</a> in the Synoptic Gospels and in the Pauline writings on the Last Supper.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFreedman2000792_204-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFreedman2000792-204">[192]</a></sup></p>
<p>In all four gospels, Jesus predicts that Peter will deny knowledge of him three times before the <a href="/wiki/Rooster" title="Rooster">rooster</a> crows the next morning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Denial_205-0"><a href="#cite_note-Denial-205">[193]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[194]</a></sup> In Luke and John, the prediction is made during the Supper (<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(American_Standard)/Luke#22:33" title="s:Bible (American Standard)/Luke">Luke 22:34</a>, <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(American_Standard)/John#22:33" title="s:Bible (American Standard)/John">John 22:34</a>). In Matthew and Mark, the prediction is made after the Supper; Jesus also predicts that all his disciples will desert him (<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(American_Standard)/Matthew#26:31" title="s:Bible (American Standard)/Matthew">Matthew 26:31–34</a>, <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(American_Standard)/Mark#14:27" title="s:Bible (American Standard)/Mark">Mark 14:27–30</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWalvoordZuck198383.E2.80.9385_207-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWalvoordZuck198383.E2.80.9385-207">[195]</a></sup> The Gospel of John provides the only account of <a href="/wiki/Foot_washing" title="Foot washing">Jesus washing his disciples' feet</a> after the meal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarris1985272.E2.80.9385_129-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarris1985272.E2.80.9385-129">[117]</a></sup> John also includes a long sermon by Jesus, preparing his disciples (now without Judas) for his departure. <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(American_Standard)/John#14:1" title="s:Bible (American Standard)/John">Chapters 14–17</a> of the Gospel of John are known as the <a href="/wiki/Farewell_Discourse" title="Farewell Discourse">Farewell Discourse</a> and are a significant source of <a href="/wiki/Christology" title="Christology">Christological</a> content.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gail142_208-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gail142-208">[196]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Herman546_209-0"><a href="#cite_note-Herman546-209">[197]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span id="Agony_in_the_Garden,_betrayal,_and_arrest"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Agony_in_the_Garden.2C_betrayal.2C_and_arrest">Agony in the Garden, betrayal, and arrest</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Agony_in_the_Garden" title="Agony in the Garden">Agony in the Garden</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kiss_of_Judas" title="Kiss of Judas">Kiss of Judas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arrest_of_Jesus" title="Arrest of Jesus">Arrest of Jesus</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Malchus" title="Malchus">Malchus</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Caravaggio_-_Taking_of_Christ_-_Dublin_-_2.jpg"><img alt="Judas kisses Jesus, and soldiers rush to seize the latter." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1378" data-file-width="1800" height="168" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Caravaggio_-_Taking_of_Christ_-_Dublin_-_2.jpg/220px-Caravaggio_-_Taking_of_Christ_-_Dublin_-_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Caravaggio_-_Taking_of_Christ_-_Dublin_-_2.jpg/330px-Caravaggio_-_Taking_of_Christ_-_Dublin_-_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Caravaggio_-_Taking_of_Christ_-_Dublin_-_2.jpg/440px-Caravaggio_-_Taking_of_Christ_-_Dublin_-_2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Caravaggio_-_Taking_of_Christ_-_Dublin_-_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A depiction of the <a href="/wiki/Kiss_of_Judas" title="Kiss of Judas">kiss of Judas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arrest_of_Jesus" title="Arrest of Jesus">arrest of Jesus</a>, by <a href="/wiki/Caravaggio" title="Caravaggio">Caravaggio</a>, c. 1602</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the Synoptics, Jesus and his disciples go to the garden <a href="/wiki/Gethsemane" title="Gethsemane">Gethsemane</a>, where Jesus prays to be spared his coming ordeal. Then Judas comes with an armed mob, sent by the chief priests, scribes and elders. He <a href="/wiki/Kiss_of_Judas" title="Kiss of Judas">kisses Jesus</a> to identify him to the crowd, which then <a href="/wiki/Arrest_of_Jesus" title="Arrest of Jesus">arrests Jesus</a>. In an attempt to stop them, an unnamed disciple of Jesus uses a sword to cut off the ear of a man in the crowd. After Jesus' arrest, his disciples go into hiding, and Peter, when questioned, thrice <a href="/wiki/Denial_of_Peter" title="Denial of Peter">denies</a> knowing Jesus. After the third denial, Peter hears the rooster crow and recalls Jesus' prediction about his denial. Peter then weeps bitterly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWalvoordZuck198383.E2.80.9385_207-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWalvoordZuck198383.E2.80.9385-207">[195]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarris1985285.E2.80.9396_148-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarris1985285.E2.80.9396-148">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Denial_205-1"><a href="#cite_note-Denial-205">[193]</a></sup></p>
<p>In John (18:1–11), Jesus does not pray to be spared his crucifixion, as the gospel portrays him as scarcely touched by such human weakness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrossLivingstone2005Jesus_210-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrossLivingstone2005Jesus-210">[198]</a></sup> The people who arrest him are Roman soldiers and Temple guards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[199]</a></sup> Instead of being betrayed by a kiss, Jesus proclaims his identity, and when he does, the soldiers and officers fall to the ground. The gospel identifies Peter as the disciple who used the sword, and Jesus rebukes him for it.</p>
<h4><span id="Trials_by_the_Sanhedrin,_Herod,_and_Pilate"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Trials_by_the_Sanhedrin.2C_Herod.2C_and_Pilate">Trials by the Sanhedrin, Herod, and Pilate</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Sanhedrin_trial_of_Jesus" title="Sanhedrin trial of Jesus">Sanhedrin trial of Jesus</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pilate%27s_Court" title="Pilate's Court">Pilate's Court</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jesus_at_Herod%27s_Court" title="Jesus at Herod's Court">Jesus at Herod's Court</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crown_of_Thorns" title="Crown of Thorns">Crown of Thorns</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Jesus,_King_of_the_Jews" title="Jesus, King of the Jews">Jesus, King of the Jews</a>; <a href="/wiki/John_18:38" title="John 18:38">John 18:38</a>; and <a href="/wiki/Ecce_homo" title="Ecce homo">Ecce homo</a></div>
<p>After his arrest, Jesus is taken to the <a href="/wiki/Sanhedrin" title="Sanhedrin">Sanhedrin</a>, a Jewish judicial body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrown1997146_212-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrown1997146-212">[200]</a></sup> The gospel accounts differ on the details of the trials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[201]</a></sup> In <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(American_Standard)/Matthew#26:57" title="s:Bible (American Standard)/Matthew">Matthew 26:57</a>, <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(American_Standard)/Mark#14:53" title="s:Bible (American Standard)/Mark">Mark 14:53</a> and <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(American_Standard)/Luke#22:54" title="s:Bible (American Standard)/Luke">Luke 22:54</a>, Jesus is taken to the house of the high priest, <a href="/wiki/Caiaphas" title="Caiaphas">Caiaphas</a>, where he is <a href="/wiki/Mocking_of_Jesus" title="Mocking of Jesus">mocked</a> and beaten that night. Early the next morning, the chief priests and scribes lead Jesus away into their council.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans2003487.E2.80.93500_214-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans2003487.E2.80.93500-214">[202]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlomberg2009396.E2.80.93400_215-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlomberg2009396.E2.80.93400-215">[203]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holman608_216-0"><a href="#cite_note-Holman608-216">[204]</a></sup> <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(American_Standard)/John#18:12" title="s:Bible (American Standard)/John">John 18:12–14</a> states that Jesus is first taken to <a href="/wiki/Annas" title="Annas">Annas</a>, Caiaphas' father-in-law, and then to the high priest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans2003487.E2.80.93500_214-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans2003487.E2.80.93500-214">[202]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlomberg2009396.E2.80.93400_215-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlomberg2009396.E2.80.93400-215">[203]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holman608_216-1"><a href="#cite_note-Holman608-216">[204]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ecce_homo_by_Antonio_Ciseri_(1).jpg"><img alt="A depiction of Jesus' public trial" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="612" data-file-width="799" height="199" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Ecce_homo_by_Antonio_Ciseri_%281%29.jpg/260px-Ecce_homo_by_Antonio_Ciseri_%281%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Ecce_homo_by_Antonio_Ciseri_%281%29.jpg/390px-Ecce_homo_by_Antonio_Ciseri_%281%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Ecce_homo_by_Antonio_Ciseri_%281%29.jpg/520px-Ecce_homo_by_Antonio_Ciseri_%281%29.jpg 2x" width="260"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ecce_homo_by_Antonio_Ciseri_(1).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Ecce homo!</i> <a href="/wiki/Antonio_Ciseri" title="Antonio Ciseri">Antonio Ciseri</a>'s 1871 depiction of <a href="/wiki/Pontius_Pilate" title="Pontius Pilate">Pontius Pilate</a> presenting Jesus to the public</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During the trials Jesus speaks very little, mounts no defense, and gives very infrequent and indirect answers to the priests' questions, prompting an officer to slap him. In <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(American_Standard)/Matthew#26:62" title="s:Bible (American Standard)/Matthew">Matthew 26:62</a> Jesus' unresponsiveness leads Caiaphas to ask him, "Have you no answer?"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans2003487.E2.80.93500_214-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans2003487.E2.80.93500-214">[202]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlomberg2009396.E2.80.93400_215-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlomberg2009396.E2.80.93400-215">[203]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holman608_216-2"><a href="#cite_note-Holman608-216">[204]</a></sup> In <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(American_Standard)/Mark#14:61" title="s:Bible (American Standard)/Mark">Mark 14:61</a> the high priest then asks Jesus, "Are you the Messiah, the Son of the Blessed One?" Jesus replies, "I am", and then predicts the coming of the <a href="/wiki/Son_of_man_(Christianity)" title="Son of man (Christianity)">Son of Man</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_53-6"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-53">[43]</a></sup> This provokes Caiaphas to tear his own robe in anger and to accuse Jesus of <a href="/wiki/Blasphemy" title="Blasphemy">blasphemy</a>. In Matthew and Luke, Jesus' answer is more ambiguous:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_53-7"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-53">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans2003495_217-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans2003495-217">[205]</a></sup> in <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(American_Standard)/Matthew#26:64" title="s:Bible (American Standard)/Matthew">Matthew 26:64</a> he responds, "You have said so", and in <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(American_Standard)/Luke#22:70" title="s:Bible (American Standard)/Luke">Luke 22:70</a> he says, "You say that I am".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlomberg2009396.E2.80.9398_218-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlomberg2009396.E2.80.9398-218">[206]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219"><a href="#cite_note-219">[207]</a></sup></p>
<p>They take Jesus to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pilate%27s_Court" title="Pilate's Court">Pilate's Court</a>, but <a href="/wiki/Pontius_Pilate" title="Pontius Pilate">Pontius Pilate</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Roman_administration_of_Judaea_(AD_6%E2%80%93135)" title="Roman administration of Judaea (AD 6–135)">Prefect of Judaea</a>, proves extremely reluctant to condemn Jesus; according to <a href="/wiki/Robert_W._Funk" title="Robert W. Funk">Robert W. Funk</a>, it is the Jewish elders who are to blame for Jesus' crucifixion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ActJMark_220-0"><a href="#cite_note-ActJMark-220">[208]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Augustine_of_Hippo" title="Augustine of Hippo">Augustine of Hippo</a> says that Pilate was not free from blame, since he exercised his power to execute Jesus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-221"><a href="#cite_note-221">[209]</a></sup> The Jewish elders ask the Roman governor Pontius Pilate to judge and condemn Jesus, accusing him of claiming to be the King of the Jews.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holman608_216-3"><a href="#cite_note-Holman608-216">[204]</a></sup> The use of the word "king" is central to the discussion between Jesus and Pilate. In <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(American_Standard)/John#18:36" title="s:Bible (American Standard)/John">John 18:36</a> Jesus states, "My kingdom is not from this world", but he does not unequivocally deny being the King of the Jews.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222"><a href="#cite_note-222">[210]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[211]</a></sup> In <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(American_Standard)/Luke#23:7" title="s:Bible (American Standard)/Luke">Luke 23:7–15</a> Pilate realizes that Jesus is a Galilean, and thus comes under the jurisdiction of <a href="/wiki/Herod_Antipas" title="Herod Antipas">Herod Antipas</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Herodian_Tetrarchy" title="Herodian Tetrarchy">Tetrarch</a> of Galilee and Perea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENiswonger1992172_224-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENiswonger1992172-224">[212]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMajern.C3.ADkPonessaManhardt2005181_225-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMajern.C3.ADkPonessaManhardt2005181-225">[213]</a></sup> Pilate sends Jesus to Herod to be tried,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarter2003120.E2.80.93121_226-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarter2003120.E2.80.93121-226">[214]</a></sup> but Jesus says almost nothing in response to Herod's questions. Herod and his soldiers mock Jesus, put an expensive robe on him to make him look like a king, and return him to Pilate,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENiswonger1992172_224-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENiswonger1992172-224">[212]</a></sup> who then calls together the Jewish elders and announces that he has "not found this man guilty".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarter2003120.E2.80.9321_227-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarter2003120.E2.80.9321-227">[215]</a></sup></p>
<p>Observing a <a href="/wiki/Passover" title="Passover">Passover</a> custom of the time, Pilate allows one prisoner chosen by the crowd to be released. He gives the people a choice between Jesus and a murderer called <a href="/wiki/Barabbas" title="Barabbas">Barabbas</a>. Persuaded by the elders (<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(American_Standard)/Matthew#27:20" title="s:Bible (American Standard)/Matthew">Matthew 27:20</a>), the mob chooses to release Barabbas and crucify Jesus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlomberg2009400.E2.80.9301_228-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlomberg2009400.E2.80.9301-228">[216]</a></sup> Pilate writes a sign in Hebrew, Latin, and Greek that reads "Jesus of Nazareth, the King of the Jews" (abbreviated as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/INRI" title="INRI">INRI</a> in depictions) to be affixed to Jesus' cross (<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(American_Standard)/John#19:19" title="s:Bible (American Standard)/John">John 19:19–20</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrown198893_229-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrown198893-229">[217]</a></sup> then <a href="/wiki/Flagellation_of_Christ" title="Flagellation of Christ">scourges Jesus</a> and sends him to be crucified. The soldiers place a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crown_of_Thorns" title="Crown of Thorns">Crown of Thorns</a> on Jesus' head and ridicule him as the King of the Jews. They beat and taunt him before taking him to <a href="/wiki/Calvary" title="Calvary">Calvary</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Senior_230-0"><a href="#cite_note-Senior-230">[218]</a></sup> also called Golgotha, for crucifixion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans2003487.E2.80.93500_214-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans2003487.E2.80.93500-214">[202]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holman608_216-4"><a href="#cite_note-Holman608-216">[204]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlomberg2009402_231-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlomberg2009402-231">[219]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Crucifixion_and_entombment">Crucifixion and entombment</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pietro_Perugino_040.jpg"><img alt="A depiction of Jesus on the cross" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3751" data-file-width="2024" height="315" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Pietro_Perugino_040.jpg/170px-Pietro_Perugino_040.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Pietro_Perugino_040.jpg/255px-Pietro_Perugino_040.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Pietro_Perugino_040.jpg/340px-Pietro_Perugino_040.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pietro_Perugino_040.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Pietro_Perugino" title="Pietro Perugino">Pietro Perugino</a>'s depiction of the <a href="/wiki/Crucifixion_of_Jesus" title="Crucifixion of Jesus">Crucifixion</a> as <i><a href="/wiki/Stabat_Mater_(art)" title="Stabat Mater (art)">Stabat Mater</a></i>, 1482</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Crucifixion_of_Jesus" title="Crucifixion of Jesus">Crucifixion of Jesus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Burial_of_Jesus" title="Burial of Jesus">Burial of Jesus</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Sayings_of_Jesus_on_the_cross" title="Sayings of Jesus on the cross">Sayings of Jesus on the cross</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crucifixion_eclipse" title="Crucifixion eclipse">Crucifixion eclipse</a></div>
<p>Jesus' crucifixion is described in all four canonical gospels. After the trials, Jesus is led to <a href="/wiki/Calvary" title="Calvary">Calvary</a> <a href="/wiki/Christ_Carrying_the_Cross" title="Christ Carrying the Cross">carrying his cross</a>; the route traditionally thought to have been taken is known as the <a href="/wiki/Via_Dolorosa" title="Via Dolorosa">Via Dolorosa</a>. The three Synoptic Gospels indicate that <a href="/wiki/Simon_of_Cyrene" title="Simon of Cyrene">Simon of Cyrene</a> assists him, having been compelled by the Romans to do so.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans2003509.E2.80.9320_232-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans2003509.E2.80.9320-232">[220]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEK.C3.B6stenbergerKellumQuarles2009211.E2.80.9314_233-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEK.C3.B6stenbergerKellumQuarles2009211.E2.80.9314-233">[221]</a></sup> In <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(American_Standard)/Luke#23:27" title="s:Bible (American Standard)/Luke">Luke 23:27–28</a> Jesus tells the women in the multitude of people following him not to weep for him but for themselves and their children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans2003509.E2.80.9320_232-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans2003509.E2.80.9320-232">[220]</a></sup> At Calvary, Jesus is offered a concoction usually offered as a <a href="/wiki/Analgesic" title="Analgesic">painkiller</a>. According to Matthew and Mark, he refuses it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans2003509.E2.80.9320_232-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans2003509.E2.80.9320-232">[220]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEK.C3.B6stenbergerKellumQuarles2009211.E2.80.9314_233-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEK.C3.B6stenbergerKellumQuarles2009211.E2.80.9314-233">[221]</a></sup></p>
<p>The soldiers then crucify Jesus and <a href="/wiki/Sortes_(ancient_Rome)" title="Sortes (ancient Rome)">cast lots</a> for his clothes. Above Jesus' head on the cross is Pilate's inscription, "Jesus of Nazareth, the King of the Jews." Soldiers and passersby <a href="/wiki/Mocking_of_Jesus" title="Mocking of Jesus">mock</a> him about it. Two convicted thieves are crucified along with Jesus. In Matthew and Mark, both thieves mock Jesus. In Luke, <a href="/wiki/Impenitent_thief" title="Impenitent thief">one of them</a> rebukes Jesus, while <a href="/wiki/Penitent_thief" title="Penitent thief">the other</a> defends him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans2003509.E2.80.9320_232-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans2003509.E2.80.9320-232">[220]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoninger1999271_234-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoninger1999271-234">[222]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-235"><a href="#cite_note-235">[223]</a></sup> Jesus tells the latter: "today you will be with me in Paradise" (Luke 23:43). In John, Mary, the mother of Jesus, and the beloved disciple were at the crucifixion. Jesus tells the beloved disciple to take of his mother (John 19:26–27).</p>
<p>The Roman soldiers break the two thieves' legs (a procedure designed to hasten death in a crucifixion), but they do not break those of Jesus, as he is already dead (John 19:33). In <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(American_Standard)/John_19:34" title="s:Bible (American Standard)/John 19:34">John 19:34</a>, <a href="/wiki/Longinus" title="Longinus">one soldier</a> pierces Jesus' side with a <a href="/wiki/Holy_Lance" title="Holy Lance">lance</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Five_Holy_Wounds" title="Five Holy Wounds">blood and water</a> flow out.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoninger1999.5Bhttps:.2F.2Fbooks.google.com.2Fbooks.3Fid.3DZP_f9icf2roC.26pg.3DPA271.23v.3Donepage.26q.3D.2522already.2520dead.2522.2520.2522blood.2520and.2520water.2522.26f.3Dfalse_271.5D_236-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoninger1999.5Bhttps:.2F.2Fbooks.google.com.2Fbooks.3Fid.3DZP_f9icf2roC.26pg.3DPA271.23v.3Donepage.26q.3D.2522already.2520dead.2522.2520.2522blood.2520and.2520water.2522.26f.3Dfalse_271.5D-236">[224]</a></sup> In the Synoptics, when Jesus dies, the heavy curtain at the Temple is torn. In <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(American_Standard)/Matthew#27:51" title="s:Bible (American Standard)/Matthew">Matthew 27:51–54</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crucifixion_darkness" title="Crucifixion darkness">an earthquake breaks open tombs</a>. In Matthew and Mark, terrified by the events, a Roman <a href="/wiki/Centurion" title="Centurion">centurion</a> states that Jesus was the <a href="/wiki/Son_of_God_(Christianity)" title="Son of God (Christianity)">Son of God</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans2003509.E2.80.9320_232-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans2003509.E2.80.9320-232">[220]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEK.C3.B6stenbergerKellumQuarles2009213.E2.80.9314_237-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEK.C3.B6stenbergerKellumQuarles2009213.E2.80.9314-237">[225]</a></sup></p>
<p>On the same day, <a href="/wiki/Joseph_of_Arimathea" title="Joseph of Arimathea">Joseph of Arimathea</a>, with Pilate's permission and with <a href="/wiki/Nicodemus" title="Nicodemus">Nicodemus</a>' help, <a href="/wiki/Descent_from_the_Cross" title="Descent from the Cross">removes Jesus' body from the cross</a>, wraps him in a clean cloth, and buries him in his new <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sepulchre" title="Sepulchre">rock-hewn tomb</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans2003509.E2.80.9320_232-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans2003509.E2.80.9320-232">[220]</a></sup> In <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(American_Standard)/Matthew#27:62" title="s:Bible (American Standard)/Matthew">Matthew 27:62–66</a>, on the following day the chief Jewish priests ask Pilate for the tomb to be secured, and with Pilate's permission the priests place seals on the large stone covering the entrance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans2003509.E2.80.9320_232-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans2003509.E2.80.9320-232">[220]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorris1992727_238-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorris1992727-238">[226]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Resurrection_and_Ascension">Resurrection and Ascension</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:IVANOV_YAV_HRISTA_MARI1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="855" data-file-width="1134" height="196" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/IVANOV_YAV_HRISTA_MARI1.jpg/260px-IVANOV_YAV_HRISTA_MARI1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/IVANOV_YAV_HRISTA_MARI1.jpg/390px-IVANOV_YAV_HRISTA_MARI1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/IVANOV_YAV_HRISTA_MARI1.jpg/520px-IVANOV_YAV_HRISTA_MARI1.jpg 2x" width="260"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:IVANOV_YAV_HRISTA_MARI1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Depiction of the resurrected Christ</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Resurrection_of_Jesus" title="Resurrection of Jesus">Resurrection of Jesus</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Post-resurrection_appearances_of_Jesus" title="Post-resurrection appearances of Jesus">Post-resurrection appearances of Jesus</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ascension_of_Jesus" title="Ascension of Jesus">Ascension of Jesus</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Empty_tomb" title="Empty tomb">Empty tomb</a>, <a href="/wiki/Great_Commission" title="Great Commission">Great Commission</a>, <a href="/wiki/Second_Coming" title="Second Coming">Second Coming</a>, <a href="/wiki/Resurrection_of_Jesus_in_Christian_art" title="Resurrection of Jesus in Christian art">Resurrection of Jesus in Christian art</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ascension_of_Jesus_in_Christian_art" title="Ascension of Jesus in Christian art">Ascension of Jesus in Christian art</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Mary_Magdalene" title="Mary Magdalene">Mary Magdalene</a> (alone in John, but accompanied by other women in the Synoptics) goes to Jesus' tomb on Sunday morning and is surprised to find it empty. Despite Jesus' teaching, the disciples had not understood that Jesus would rise again.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarris1985308.E2.80.9309_239-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarris1985308.E2.80.9309-239">[227]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>In Matthew, there are guards at the tomb. An angel descends from heaven, and opens the tomb. The guards faint from fear. Jesus appears to Mary Magdalene and "the other Mary" after they visited the tomb. Jesus then appears to the eleven remaining disciples in Galilee and <a href="/wiki/Great_Commission" title="Great Commission">commissions them</a> to baptize all nations in the name of the Father, Son and Holy Spirit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarris1985272.E2.80.9385_129-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarris1985272.E2.80.9385-129">[117]</a></sup></li>
<li>In Mark, <a href="/wiki/Salome_(disciple)" title="Salome (disciple)">Salome</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mary,_mother_of_James" title="Mary, mother of James">Mary, mother of James</a> are with Mary Magdalene (Mark 16:1). In the tomb, a young man in a white robe (an angel) tells them that Jesus will meet his disciples in Galilee, as he had told them (referring to Mark 14:28).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-May_Metzger_Mark_87-4"><a href="#cite_note-May_Metzger_Mark-87">[75]</a></sup></li>
<li>In Luke, Mary and various other women meet two angels at the tomb, but the eleven disciples do not believe their story (Luke 25:1–12). Jesus appears to two of his followers in Emmaus. He also makes an appearance to Peter. Jesus then appears that same day to his disciples in Jerusalem (Luke 24:13–43). Although he appears and vanishes mysteriously, he also eats and lets them touch him to prove that he is not a spirit. He repeats his command to bring his teaching to all nations (<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(American_Standard)/Luke#24:51" title="s:Bible (American Standard)/Luke">Luke 24:51</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarris1985297.E2.80.93301_240-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarris1985297.E2.80.93301-240">[228]</a></sup></li>
<li>In John, Mary is alone at first, but Peter and the beloved disciple come and see the tomb as well. Jesus then appears to Mary at the tomb. He later appears to the disciples, breathes on them, and gives them the power to forgive and retain sins. In a second visit to disciples, he proves to a doubting disciple ("<a href="/wiki/Doubting_Thomas" title="Doubting Thomas">Doubting Thomas</a>") that he is flesh and blood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarris1985302.E2.80.9310_115-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarris1985302.E2.80.9310-115">[103]</a></sup> The disciples return to Galilee, where Jesus makes another appearance. He performs a miracle known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catch_of_153_fish" title="Catch of 153 fish">catch of 153 fish</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Sea_of_Galilee" title="Sea of Galilee">Sea of Galilee</a>, after which Jesus encourages Peter to serve his followers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans2003521.E2.80.9330_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans2003521.E2.80.9330-80">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoxEasley2007216.E2.80.9326_241-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoxEasley2007216.E2.80.9326-241">[229]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<p>Jesus' Ascension into Heaven is described in <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(American_Standard)/Luke#24:50" title="s:Bible (American Standard)/Luke">Luke 24:50–53</a>, <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(American_Standard)/Acts#1:1" title="s:Bible (American Standard)/Acts">Acts 1:1–11</a> and mentioned in <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(American_Standard)/1_Timothy#3:16" title="s:Bible (American Standard)/1 Timothy">1 Timothy 3:16</a>. In the <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_the_Apostles" title="Acts of the Apostles">Acts of the Apostles</a>, forty days after the Resurrection, as the disciples look on, "he was lifted up, and a cloud took him out of their sight". <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(American_Standard)/1_Peter#3:22" title="s:Bible (American Standard)/1 Peter">1 Peter 3:22</a> states that Jesus has "gone into heaven and is at the right hand of God".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans2003521.E2.80.9330_80-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans2003521.E2.80.9330-80">[69]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Acts of the Apostles describes several appearances of Jesus after his Ascension. In <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(American_Standard)/Acts#7:55" title="s:Bible (American Standard)/Acts">Acts 7:55</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saint_Stephen" title="Saint Stephen">Stephen</a> gazes into heaven and sees "Jesus standing at the right hand of God" just before his death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-242"><a href="#cite_note-242">[230]</a></sup> On the road to <a href="/wiki/Damascus" title="Damascus">Damascus</a>, the Apostle <a href="/wiki/Conversion_of_Paul_the_Apostle" title="Conversion of Paul the Apostle">Paul is converted</a> to Christianity after seeing a blinding light and hearing a voice saying, "I am Jesus, whom you are persecuting" (<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(American_Standard)/Acts#9:5" title="s:Bible (American Standard)/Acts">Acts 9:5</a>). In <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(American_Standard)/Acts#9:10" title="s:Bible (American Standard)/Acts">Acts 9:10–18</a>, Jesus instructs <a href="/wiki/Ananias_of_Damascus" title="Ananias of Damascus">Ananias of Damascus</a> in a vision to heal Paul.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HarringtonActs_243-0"><a href="#cite_note-HarringtonActs-243">[231]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Revelation" title="Book of Revelation">Book of Revelation</a> includes a revelation from Jesus concerning the <a href="/wiki/Christian_eschatology" title="Christian eschatology">last days</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-244"><a href="#cite_note-244">[232]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_views">Historical views</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Historical_Jesus" title="Historical Jesus">Historical Jesus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Quest_for_the_historical_Jesus" title="Quest for the historical Jesus">Quest for the historical Jesus</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Biblical_criticism" title="Biblical criticism">Biblical criticism</a></div>
<p>Prior to the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Enlightenment</a>, the gospels were usually regarded as accurate historical accounts, but since then scholars have emerged who question the reliability of the gospels and draw a distinction between the Jesus described in the gospels and the Jesus of history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELevine20065_245-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELevine20065-245">[233]</a></sup> Since the 18th century, three separate scholarly quests for the historical Jesus have taken place, each with distinct characteristics and based on different research criteria, which were often developed during the quest that applied them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWitherington1997113_109-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWitherington1997113-109">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPowell199819.E2.80.9323_246-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPowell199819.E2.80.9323-246">[234]</a></sup> While there is widespread scholarly agreement on the existence of Jesus,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-exist_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-exist-27">[g]</a></sup> and a basic consensus on the general outline of his life,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-248"><a href="#cite_note-248">[m]</a></sup> the portraits of Jesus constructed by various scholars often differ from each other, and from the image portrayed in the gospel accounts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-249"><a href="#cite_note-249">[236]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-250"><a href="#cite_note-250">[237]</a></sup></p>
<p>Approaches to the historical reconstruction of the life of Jesus have varied from the "maximalist" approaches of the 19th century, in which the gospel accounts were accepted as reliable evidence wherever it is possible, to the "minimalist" approaches of the early 20th century, where hardly anything about Jesus was accepted as historical.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-251"><a href="#cite_note-251">[238]</a></sup> In the 1950s, as the second quest for the historical Jesus gathered pace, the minimalist approaches faded away, and in the 21st century, minimalists such as <a href="/wiki/Robert_M._Price" title="Robert M. Price">Price</a> are a very small minority.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChiltonEvans199827_252-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChiltonEvans199827-252">[239]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans20124.E2.80.935_253-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans20124.E2.80.935-253">[240]</a></sup> Although a belief in the inerrancy of the gospels cannot be supported historically, many scholars since the 1980s have held that, beyond the few facts considered to be historically certain, certain other elements of Jesus' life are "historically probable".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChiltonEvans199827_252-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChiltonEvans199827-252">[239]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-254"><a href="#cite_note-254">[241]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETheissenWinter2002142.E2.80.9343_255-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETheissenWinter2002142.E2.80.9343-255">[242]</a></sup> Modern scholarly research on the historical Jesus thus focuses on identifying the most probable elements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-256"><a href="#cite_note-256">[243]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeier2006124_257-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeier2006124-257">[244]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Judea_and_Galilee_in_the_1st_century">Judea and Galilee in the 1st century</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Palestine_in_the_time_of_Jesus.jpg"><img alt="A map. See description" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2573" data-file-width="1826" height="310" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Palestine_in_the_time_of_Jesus.jpg/220px-Palestine_in_the_time_of_Jesus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Palestine_in_the_time_of_Jesus.jpg/330px-Palestine_in_the_time_of_Jesus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Palestine_in_the_time_of_Jesus.jpg/440px-Palestine_in_the_time_of_Jesus.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Palestine_in_the_time_of_Jesus.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Judea" title="Judea">Judea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Galilee" title="Galilee">Galilee</a> and neighboring areas at the time of Jesus</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In AD 6, <a href="/wiki/Judea" title="Judea">Judea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Edom" title="Edom">Idumea</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Samaria" title="Samaria">Samaria</a> were transformed from a <a href="/wiki/Client_state" title="Client state">client kingdom</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a> into an imperial province, also called <a href="/wiki/Judea_(Roman_province)" title="Judea (Roman province)">Judea</a>. A Roman <a href="/wiki/Prefect" title="Prefect">prefect</a>, rather than a client king, ruled the land. The prefect ruled from <a href="/wiki/Caesarea_Maritima" title="Caesarea Maritima">Caesarea Maritima</a>, leaving <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem" title="Jerusalem">Jerusalem</a> to be run by the <a href="/wiki/List_of_High_Priests_of_Israel" title="List of High Priests of Israel">High Priest of Israel</a>. As an exception, the prefect came to Jerusalem during religious festivals, when religious and patriotic enthusiasm sometimes inspired unrest or uprisings. Gentile lands surrounded the Jewish territories of Judea and <a href="/wiki/Galilee" title="Galilee">Galilee</a>, but Roman law and practice allowed Jews to remain separate legally and culturally. Galilee was evidently prosperous, and poverty was limited enough that it did not threaten the social order.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_53-8"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-53">[43]</a></sup></p>
<p>Jewish religion was unusual in that Jews acknowledged only one God, they considered themselves chosen by him, and they wanted <a href="/wiki/Gentile" title="Gentile">Gentiles</a> to accept their God as the only God. Jews based their faith and religious practice on the <a href="/wiki/Torah" title="Torah">Torah</a>, five books said to have been given by God to <a href="/wiki/Moses" title="Moses">Moses</a>. The three prominent religious parties were the <a href="/wiki/Pharisees" title="Pharisees">Pharisees</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Essenes" title="Essenes">Essenes</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Sadducees" title="Sadducees">Sadducees</a>. Together these parties represented only a small fraction of the population. Most Jews looked forward to a time that God would deliver them from their pagan rulers, possibly through war against the Romans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_53-9"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-53">[43]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sources">Sources</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sources_for_the_historicity_of_Jesus" title="Sources for the historicity of Jesus">Sources for the historicity of Jesus</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Josephus_on_Jesus" title="Josephus on Jesus">Josephus on Jesus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tacitus_on_Christ" title="Tacitus on Christ">Tacitus on Christ</a></div>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;max-width:302px">
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:146px;max-width:146px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Josephusbust.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="401" height="215" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Josephusbust.jpg/144px-Josephusbust.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Josephusbust.jpg/216px-Josephusbust.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Josephusbust.jpg/288px-Josephusbust.jpg 2x" width="144"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:152px;max-width:152px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:WorksJosephus1640TP.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1101" data-file-width="772" height="214" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/WorksJosephus1640TP.jpg/150px-WorksJosephus1640TP.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/WorksJosephus1640TP.jpg/225px-WorksJosephus1640TP.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/WorksJosephus1640TP.jpg/300px-WorksJosephus1640TP.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a></div>
</div>
<div style="clear:left"></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent">A 1640 edition of the works of Josephus, a 1st century <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Jews" title="Roman Jews">Roman-Jewish</a> historian who referred to Jesus<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlomberg2009431.E2.80.9336_258-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlomberg2009431.E2.80.9336-258">[245]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>New Testament scholars face a formidable challenge when they analyze the canonical Gospels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarris1985263_259-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarris1985263-259">[246]</a></sup> The Gospels are not biographies in the modern sense, and the authors explain Jesus' theological significance and recount his public ministry while omitting many details of his life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarris1985263_259-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarris1985263-259">[246]</a></sup> The reports of supernatural events associated with Jesus' death and resurrection make the challenge even more difficult.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarris1985263_259-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarris1985263-259">[246]</a></sup> Scholars regard the gospels as compromised sources of information because the writers were trying to glorify Jesus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESanders19933_96-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESanders19933-96">[84]</a></sup> Even so, the sources for Jesus' life are better than sources scholars have for the life of <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESanders19933_96-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESanders19933-96">[84]</a></sup> Scholars use a number of criteria, such as the <a href="/wiki/Criterion_of_multiple_attestation" title="Criterion of multiple attestation">criterion of independent attestation</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Biblical_criticism#Coherence" title="Biblical criticism">criterion of coherence</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Criterion_of_discontinuity" title="Criterion of discontinuity">criterion of discontinuity</a> to judge the historicity of events.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERausch200336.E2.80.9337_260-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERausch200336.E2.80.9337-260">[247]</a></sup> The historicity of an event also depends on the reliability of the source; indeed, the gospels are not independent nor consistent records of Jesus' life. Mark, which is most likely the earliest written gospel, has been considered for many decades the most historically accurate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-261"><a href="#cite_note-261">[248]</a></sup> John, the latest written gospel, differs considerably from the Synoptic Gospels, and thus is generally considered less reliable, although more and more scholars now also recognize that it may contain a core of older material as historically valuable as the Synoptic tradition or even more so.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-262"><a href="#cite_note-262">[249]</a></sup></p>
<p>The non-canonical <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_Thomas" title="Gospel of Thomas">Gospel of Thomas</a> might be an independent witness to many of Jesus' parables and aphorisms. For example, Thomas confirms that Jesus blessed the poor and that this saying circulated independently before being combined with similar sayings in the <a href="/wiki/Q_source" title="Q source">Q source</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFunkHoover1993471.E2.80.93532_263-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFunkHoover1993471.E2.80.93532-263">[250]</a></sup> Other select non-canonical Christian texts may also have value for historical Jesus research.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETheissenMerz199817.E2.80.9362_101-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETheissenMerz199817.E2.80.9362-101">[89]</a></sup></p>
<p>Early non-Christian sources that attest to the historical existence of Jesus include the works of the historians <a href="/wiki/Josephus" title="Josephus">Josephus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tacitus" title="Tacitus">Tacitus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-265"><a href="#cite_note-265">[n]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlomberg2009431.E2.80.9336_258-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlomberg2009431.E2.80.9336-258">[245]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVan_Voorst200039.E2.80.9353_266-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVan_Voorst200039.E2.80.9353-266">[252]</a></sup> Josephus scholar <a href="/wiki/Louis_Feldman" title="Louis Feldman">Louis Feldman</a> has stated that "few have doubted the genuineness" of Josephus' reference to Jesus in <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/The_Antiquities_of_the_Jews/Book_XX#Chapter_9" title="s:The Antiquities of the Jews/Book XX">book 20</a> of the <i><a href="/wiki/Antiquities_of_the_Jews" title="Antiquities of the Jews">Antiquities of the Jews</a></i>, and it is disputed only by a small number of scholars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVan_Voorst200083_267-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVan_Voorst200083-267">[253]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-268"><a href="#cite_note-268">[254]</a></sup> Tacitus referred to Christ and his execution by Pilate in <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/The_Annals_(Tacitus)/Book_15#44" title="s:The Annals (Tacitus)/Book 15">book 15</a> of his work <i><a href="/wiki/Annals_(Tacitus)" title="Annals (Tacitus)">Annals</a></i>. Scholars generally consider Tacitus's reference to the execution of Jesus to be both authentic and of historical value as an independent Roman source.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-269"><a href="#cite_note-269">[255]</a></sup></p>
<p>Non-Christian sources are valuable in two ways. First, they show that even neutral or hostile parties never evince any doubt that Jesus actually existed. Second, they present a rough picture of Jesus that is compatible with that found in the Christian sources: that Jesus was a teacher, had a reputation as a miracle worker, had a brother James, and died a violent death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETheissenMerz1998_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETheissenMerz1998-14">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>Archeology helps scholars better understand Jesus' social world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReed200218_270-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReed200218-270">[256]</a></sup> Recent archeological work, for example, indicates that <a href="/wiki/Capernaum" title="Capernaum">Capernaum</a>, a city important in Jesus' ministry, was poor and small, without even a <a href="/wiki/Forum_(Roman)" title="Forum (Roman)">forum</a> or an <a href="/wiki/Agora" title="Agora">agora</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gowler_271-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gowler-271">[257]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-272"><a href="#cite_note-272">[258]</a></sup> This archaeological discovery resonates well with the scholarly view that Jesus advocated reciprocal sharing among the destitute in that area of Galilee.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gowler_271-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gowler-271">[257]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chronology">Chronology</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Chronology_of_Jesus" title="Chronology of Jesus">Chronology of Jesus</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Anno_Domini" title="Anno Domini">Anno Domini</a></div>
<p>Jesus was a Galilean Jew,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vermes_1981_18-3"><a href="#cite_note-Vermes_1981-18">[12]</a></sup> born around the beginning of the 1st century, who died in 30 or 33 AD in <a href="/wiki/Judea_(Roman_province)" title="Judea (Roman province)">Judea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Humphreys1992_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-Humphreys1992-7">[6]</a></sup> The general scholarly consensus is that Jesus was a contemporary of <a href="/wiki/John_the_Baptist" title="John the Baptist">John the Baptist</a> and was crucified by the Roman governor <a href="/wiki/Pontius_Pilate" title="Pontius Pilate">Pontius Pilate</a>, who held office from 26 to 36 AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELevine20064_35-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELevine20064-35">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p>The gospels offer several clues concerning the year of Jesus' birth. <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(American_Standard)/Matthew#2:1" title="s:Bible (American Standard)/Matthew">Matthew 2:1</a> associates the birth of Jesus with the reign of <a href="/wiki/Herod_the_Great" title="Herod the Great">Herod the Great</a>, who died around 4 BC, and <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(American_Standard)/Luke#1:5" title="s:Bible (American Standard)/Luke">Luke 1:5</a> mentions that Herod was on the throne shortly before the birth of Jesus,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaier1989115.E2.80.9318_273-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaier1989115.E2.80.9318-273">[259]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENiswonger1992121.E2.80.9322_274-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENiswonger1992121.E2.80.9322-274">[260]</a></sup> although this gospel also associates the birth with the <a href="/wiki/Census_of_Quirinius" title="Census of Quirinius">Census of Quirinius</a> which took place ten years later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEK.C3.B6stenbergerKellumQuarles2009137.E2.80.9338_275-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEK.C3.B6stenbergerKellumQuarles2009137.E2.80.9338-275">[261]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENiswonger1992122.E2.80.9324_276-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENiswonger1992122.E2.80.9324-276">[262]</a></sup> <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(American_Standard)/Luke#3:23" title="s:Bible (American Standard)/Luke">Luke 3:23</a> states that Jesus was "about thirty years old" at the start of his <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Jesus" title="Ministry of Jesus">ministry</a>, which according to <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(American_Standard)/Acts#10:37" title="s:Bible (American Standard)/Acts">Acts 10:37–38</a> was preceded by John the Baptist's ministry, itself recorded in <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(American_Standard)/Luke#3:1" title="s:Bible (American Standard)/Luke">Luke 3:1–2</a> to have begun in the 15th year of <a href="/wiki/Tiberius" title="Tiberius">Tiberius</a>' reign (28 or 29 AD).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENiswonger1992121.E2.80.9322_274-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENiswonger1992121.E2.80.9322-274">[260]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vermes2006_277-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vermes2006-277">[263]</a></sup> By collating the gospel accounts with historical data and using various other methods, most scholars arrive at a date of birth between 6 and 4 BC for Jesus,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vermes2006_277-1"><a href="#cite_note-Vermes2006-277">[263]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDunn2003324_278-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunn2003324-278">[264]</a></sup> but some propose estimates that lie in a wider range.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-279"><a href="#cite_note-279">[o]</a></sup></p>
<p>The years of Jesus' ministry have been estimated using several different approaches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEK.C3.B6stenbergerKellumQuarles2009140_280-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEK.C3.B6stenbergerKellumQuarles2009140-280">[265]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFreedman2000249_281-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFreedman2000249-281">[266]</a></sup> One of these applies the reference in <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(American_Standard)/Luke#3:1" title="s:Bible (American Standard)/Luke">Luke 3:1–2</a>, <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(American_Standard)/Acts#10:37" title="s:Bible (American Standard)/Acts">Acts 10:37–38</a> and the dates of Tiberius' reign, which are well known, to give a date of around 28–29 AD for the start of Jesus' ministry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaier1989120.E2.80.9321_282-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaier1989120.E2.80.9321-282">[267]</a></sup> Another approach uses the statement about the temple in <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(American_Standard)/John#2:13" title="s:Bible (American Standard)/John">John 2:13–20</a>, which asserts that the <a href="/wiki/Second_Temple" title="Second Temple">temple in Jerusalem</a> was in its 46th year of construction at the start of Jesus' ministry, together with <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/The_Antiquities_of_the_Jews/Book_XV" title="s:The Antiquities of the Jews/Book XV">Josephus' statement</a> that the temple's reconstruction was started by Herod the Great in the 18th year of his reign, to estimate a date around 27–29 AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEK.C3.B6stenbergerKellumQuarles2009140_280-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEK.C3.B6stenbergerKellumQuarles2009140-280">[265]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaier1989123_283-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaier1989123-283">[268]</a></sup> A further method uses the date of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beheading_of_St._John_the_Baptist" title="Beheading of St. John the Baptist">death of John the Baptist</a> and the marriage of <a href="/wiki/Herod_Antipas" title="Herod Antipas">Herod Antipas</a> to <a href="/wiki/Herodias" title="Herodias">Herodias</a>, based on the writings of Josephus, and correlates it with <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(American_Standard)/Matthew#14:4" title="s:Bible (American Standard)/Matthew">Matthew 14:4</a> and <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(American_Standard)/Mark#6:18" title="s:Bible (American Standard)/Mark">Mark 6:18</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-284"><a href="#cite_note-284">[269]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-285"><a href="#cite_note-285">[270]</a></sup> Given that most scholars date the marriage of Herod and Herodias as AD 28–35, this yields a date about 28–29 AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFreedman2000249_281-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFreedman2000249-281">[266]</a></sup></p>
<p>A number of approaches have been used to estimate the year of the crucifixion of Jesus.
