<div style="clear:left"></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent">Roman senator and historian <a href="/wiki/Tacitus" title="Tacitus">Tacitus</a> wrote of the crucifixion of Christ (Jesus) in the <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/The_Annals_(Tacitus)/Book_15#44" title="s:The Annals (Tacitus)/Book 15">Annals</a></i>, a history of the Roman Empire during the 1st century.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Scholars have reached a limited consensus on the basics of Jesus' life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-White_293-0"><a href="#cite_note-White-293">[278]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Family">Family</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Brothers_of_Jesus" title="Brothers of Jesus">Brothers of Jesus</a></div>
<p>Many scholars agree that Joseph, Jesus' father, died by the time Jesus began his ministry. Joseph is not mentioned at all in the gospels during Jesus' ministry. Joseph's death would explain why in Mark 6:3, Jesus' neighbors refer to Jesus as the "son of Mary" (sons were usually identified by their fathers).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-294"><a href="#cite_note-294">[279]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to Theissen and Merz, it is common for extraordinary <a href="/wiki/Charismatic_authority" title="Charismatic authority">charismatic leaders</a>, such as Jesus, to come into conflict with their ordinary families.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETheissenMerz1998194_295-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETheissenMerz1998194-295">[280]</a></sup> In Mark, Jesus' family comes to get him, fearing that he is mad (Mark 3:20–34), and this account is likely historical because early Christians would not have invented it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ActJMark_220-1"><a href="#cite_note-ActJMark-220">[208]</a></sup> After Jesus' death, many members of his family joined the Christian movement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETheissenMerz1998194_295-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETheissenMerz1998194-295">[280]</a></sup> Jesus' brother <a href="/wiki/James,_brother_of_Jesus" title="James, brother of Jesus">James</a> became a leader of the Jerusalem Church.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrossLivingstone2005James.2C_St._296-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrossLivingstone2005James.2C_St.-296">[281]</a></sup></p>
<p>Géza Vermes says that the doctrine of the <a href="/wiki/Virgin_birth_of_Jesus" title="Virgin birth of Jesus">virgin birth of Jesus</a> arose from theological development rather than from historical events.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVermes1981283_297-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVermes1981283-297">[282]</a></sup> Despite the widely held view that the authors of the Synoptic Gospels drew upon each other (the so-called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synoptic_problem" title="Synoptic problem">synoptic problem</a>), other scholars take it as significant that the virgin birth is <a href="/wiki/Criterion_of_multiple_attestation" title="Criterion of multiple attestation">attested</a> by two separate gospels, Matthew and Luke.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-298"><a href="#cite_note-298">[283]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-299"><a href="#cite_note-299">[284]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-300"><a href="#cite_note-300">[285]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-301"><a href="#cite_note-301">[286]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-302"><a href="#cite_note-302">[287]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-303"><a href="#cite_note-303">[288]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to E. P. Sanders, the <a href="/wiki/Nativity_of_Jesus" title="Nativity of Jesus">birth narratives</a> in Matthew and Luke are the clearest case of invention in the Gospel narratives of Jesus' life. Both accounts have Jesus born in <a href="/wiki/Bethlehem" title="Bethlehem">Bethlehem</a>, in accordance with Jewish salvation history, and both have him growing up in Nazareth. But Sanders points that the two Gospels report completely different and irreconcilable explanations for how that happened. Luke's account of a census in which everyone returned to their ancestral cities is not plausible. Matthew's account is more plausible, but the story reads as though it was invented to identify Jesus as like a new <a href="/wiki/Moses" title="Moses">Moses</a>, and the historian <a href="/wiki/Josephus" title="Josephus">Josephus</a> reports Herod the Great's brutality without ever mentioning that <a href="/wiki/Massacre_of_the_Innocents" title="Massacre of the Innocents">he massacred little boys</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESanders199385.E2.80.9388_304-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESanders199385.E2.80.9388-304">[289]</a></sup></p>
<p>Sanders says that the genealogies of Jesus are based not on historical information but on the authors' desire to show that Jesus was the universal Jewish savior.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESanders199380.E2.80.9391_120-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESanders199380.E2.80.9391-120">[108]</a></sup> In any event, once the doctrine of the virgin birth of Jesus became established, that tradition superseded the earlier tradition that he was descended from <a href="/wiki/David" title="David">David</a> through Joseph.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETheissenMerz1998196_305-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETheissenMerz1998196-305">[290]</a></sup> Luke reports that Jesus was a <a href="/wiki/Consanguinity" title="Consanguinity">blood relation</a> of <a href="/wiki/John_the_Baptist" title="John the Baptist">John the Baptist</a>, but scholars generally consider this connection to be invented.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESanders199380.E2.80.9391_120-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESanders199380.E2.80.9391-120">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ActJBirth_306-0"><a href="#cite_note-ActJBirth-306">[291]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Baptism">Baptism</span></h4>
<p>Most modern scholars consider Jesus' baptism to be a definite historical fact, along with his crucifixion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDunn2003339_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunn2003339-9">[7]</a></sup> Theologian <a href="/wiki/James_Dunn_(theologian)" title="James Dunn (theologian)">James D.G. Dunn</a> states that they "command almost universal assent" and "rank so high on the 'almost impossible to doubt or deny' scale of historical facts" that they are often the starting points for the study of the historical Jesus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDunn2003339_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunn2003339-9">[7]</a></sup> Scholars adduce the <a href="/wiki/Criterion_of_embarrassment" title="Criterion of embarrassment">criterion of embarrassment</a>, saying that early Christians would not have invented a baptism that might imply that Jesus committed <a href="/wiki/Sin" title="Sin">sins</a> and wanted to <a href="/wiki/Repentance" title="Repentance">repent</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPowell199847_307-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPowell199847-307">[292]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-308"><a href="#cite_note-308">[293]</a></sup> According to Theissen and Merz, Jesus was inspired by <a href="/wiki/John_the_Baptist" title="John the Baptist">John the Baptist</a> and took over from him many elements of his teaching.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETheissenMerz1998235_309-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETheissenMerz1998235-309">[294]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ministry_in_Galilee">Ministry in Galilee</span></h4>
<p>Most scholars hold that Jesus lived in <a href="/wiki/Galilee" title="Galilee">Galilee</a> and <a href="/wiki/Judea" title="Judea">Judea</a> and did not preach or study elsewhere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn303_310-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dunn303-310">[295]</a></sup> They agree that Jesus debated with Jewish authorities on the subject of God, performed some healings, taught in <a href="/wiki/Parable" title="Parable">parables</a> and gathered followers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELevine20064_35-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELevine20064-35">[26]</a></sup> Jesus' Jewish critics considered his ministry to be scandalous because he feasted with sinners, fraternized with women, and allowed his followers to pluck grain on the Sabbath.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5GIntro_86-18"><a href="#cite_note-5GIntro-86">[74]</a></sup> According to Sanders, it is not plausible that disagreements over how to interpret the Law of Moses and the Sabbath would have led Jewish authorities to want Jesus killed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESanders1993205.E2.80.9323_311-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESanders1993205.E2.80.9323-311">[296]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to Ehrman, Jesus taught that a coming kingdom was everyone's proper focus, not anything in this life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEhrman1999167.E2.80.9370_312-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEhrman1999167.E2.80.9370-312">[297]</a></sup> He taught about the Jewish Law, seeking its true meaning, sometimes in opposition to other traditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEhrman1999164.E2.80.9367_313-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEhrman1999164.E2.80.9367-313">[298]</a></sup> Jesus put love at the center of the Law, and following that Law was an apocalyptic necessity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEhrman1999164.E2.80.9367_313-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEhrman1999164.E2.80.9367-313">[298]</a></sup> His ethical teachings called for forgiveness, not judging others, loving enemies, and caring for the poor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEhrman1999171.E2.80.9376_314-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEhrman1999171.E2.80.9376-314">[299]</a></sup> Funk and Hoover note that typical of Jesus were <a href="/wiki/Paradox" title="Paradox">paradoxical</a> or surprising turns of phrase, such as advising one, when struck on the cheek, <a href="/wiki/Turning_the_other_cheek" title="Turning the other cheek">to offer the other cheek</a> to be struck as well (Luke 6:29).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-315"><a href="#cite_note-315">[300]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Gospels portray Jesus teaching in well-defined sessions, such as Matthew's <a href="/wiki/Sermon_on_the_Mount" title="Sermon on the Mount">Sermon on the Mount</a> or Luke's parallel <a href="/wiki/Sermon_on_the_Plain" title="Sermon on the Plain">Sermon on the Plain</a>. According to Gerd Theissen and Annette Merz, these teaching sessions include authentic teachings of Jesus, but the scenes were invented by the respective evangelists to frame these teachings, which had originally been recorded without context.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETheissenMerz199817.E2.80.9362_101-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETheissenMerz199817.E2.80.9362-101">[89]</a></sup> While Jesus' <a href="/wiki/Miracle" title="Miracle">miracles</a> fit within the social context of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_history" title="Ancient history">antiquity</a>, he defined them differently. First, he attributed them to the faith of those healed. Second, he connected them to <a href="/wiki/End_time" title="End time">end times</a> prophecy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETheissenMerz1998310_316-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETheissenMerz1998310-316">[301]</a></sup></p>
<p>Jesus chose <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apostle_(Christian)" title="Apostle (Christian)">twelve disciples</a> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESanders199310_317-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESanders199310-317">[302]</a></sup> (the "Twelve"), evidently as an <a href="/wiki/Apocalypticism" title="Apocalypticism">apocalyptic</a> message.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEhrman1999186.E2.80.9387_318-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEhrman1999186.E2.80.9387-318">[303]</a></sup> All three Synoptics mention the Twelve, although the names on Luke's list vary from those in Mark and Matthew, suggesting that Christians were not certain who all the disciples were.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEhrman1999186.E2.80.9387_318-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEhrman1999186.E2.80.9387-318">[303]</a></sup> The 12 disciples might have represented the twelve original <a href="/wiki/Twelve_Tribes_of_Israel" title="Twelve Tribes of Israel">tribes of Israel</a>, which would be restored once God's rule was instituted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEhrman1999186.E2.80.9387_318-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEhrman1999186.E2.80.9387-318">[303]</a></sup> The disciples were reportedly meant to be the rulers of the tribes in the coming Kingdom (Matthew 19:28, Luke 22:30).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEhrman1999186.E2.80.9387_318-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEhrman1999186.E2.80.9387-318">[303]</a></sup> According to Bart Ehrman, Jesus' promise that the Twelve would rule is historical, because the Twelve included <a href="/wiki/Judas_Iscariot" title="Judas Iscariot">Judas Iscariot</a>. In Ehrman's view, no Christians would have invented a line from Jesus, promising rulership to the disciple who betrayed him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEhrman1999186.E2.80.9387_318-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEhrman1999186.E2.80.9387-318">[303]</a></sup> In Mark, the disciples play hardly any role other than a negative one. While others sometimes respond to Jesus with complete faith, his disciples are puzzled and doubtful.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESanders1993123.E2.80.9324_319-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESanders1993123.E2.80.9324-319">[304]</a></sup> They serve as a <a href="/wiki/Foil_(literature)" title="Foil (literature)">foil</a> to Jesus and to other characters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESanders1993123.E2.80.9324_319-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESanders1993123.E2.80.9324-319">[304]</a></sup> The failings of the disciples are probably exaggerated in Mark, and the disciples make a better showing in Matthew and Luke.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESanders1993123.E2.80.9324_319-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESanders1993123.E2.80.9324-319">[304]</a></sup></p>
<p>Sanders says that Jesus' mission was not about <a href="/wiki/Repentance" title="Repentance">repentance</a>, although he acknowledges that this opinion is unpopular. He argues that repentance appears as a strong theme only in Luke, that repentance was <a href="/wiki/John_the_Baptist" title="John the Baptist">John the Baptist</a>'s message, and that Jesus' ministry would not have been scandalous if the sinners he ate with had been repentant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESanders1993230.E2.80.9336_320-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESanders1993230.E2.80.9336-320">[305]</a></sup> According to Theissen and Merz, Jesus taught that God was generously giving people an opportunity to repent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETheissenMerz1998336_321-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETheissenMerz1998336-321">[306]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Role">Role</span></h4>
<p>Jesus taught that an apocalyptic figure, the "<a href="/wiki/Son_of_man_(Christianity)" title="Son of man (Christianity)">Son of Man</a>", would soon come on clouds of glory to gather the elect, or chosen ones (Mark 13:24–27, Matthew 24:29–31, Luke 21:25–28). He referred to himself as a "<a href="/wiki/Son_of_man" title="Son of man">son of man</a>" in the colloquial sense of "a person", but scholars do not know whether he also meant himself when he referred to the heavenly "Son of Man". <a href="/wiki/Paul_the_Apostle" title="Paul the Apostle">Paul the Apostle</a> and other early Christians interpreted the "Son of Man" as the risen Jesus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_53-10"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-53">[43]</a></sup></p>
<p>The title <a href="/wiki/Christ_(title)" title="Christ (title)">Christ</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Messiah" title="Messiah">Messiah</a>, indicates that Jesus' followers believed him to be the anointed heir of King <a href="/wiki/David" title="David">David</a>, whom some Jews expected to save Israel. The Gospels refer to him not only as a Messiah but in the absolute form as "the Messiah" or, equivalently, "the Christ". In early Judaism, this absolute form of the title is not found, but only phrases such as "his Messiah". The tradition is ambiguous enough to leave room for debate as to whether Jesus defined his <a href="/wiki/Eschatology" title="Eschatology">eschatological</a> role as that of the Messiah.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrossLivingstone2005Messiah_322-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrossLivingstone2005Messiah-322">[307]</a></sup> The Jewish messianic tradition included many different forms, some of them focused on a Messiah figure and others not.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETheissenMerz1998533.E2.80.93540_323-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETheissenMerz1998533.E2.80.93540-323">[308]</a></sup> Based on the Christian tradition, <a href="/wiki/Gerd_Theissen" title="Gerd Theissen">Gerd Theissen</a> advances the hypothesis that Jesus saw himself in messianic terms but did not claim the title "Messiah".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETheissenMerz1998533.E2.80.93540_323-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETheissenMerz1998533.E2.80.93540-323">[308]</a></sup> Bart Ehrman argues that Jesus did consider himself to be the Messiah, albeit in the sense that he would be the king of the new political order that God would usher in,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-324"><a href="#cite_note-324">[309]</a></sup> not in the sense that most people today think of the term.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-325"><a href="#cite_note-325">[310]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Passover_and_crucifixion_in_Jerusalem">Passover and crucifixion in Jerusalem</span></h4>
<p>Around AD 30, Jesus and his followers traveled from <a href="/wiki/Galilee" title="Galilee">Galilee</a> to <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem" title="Jerusalem">Jerusalem</a> to observe <a href="/wiki/Passover" title="Passover">Passover</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESanders199310_317-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESanders199310-317">[302]</a></sup> Jesus caused a disturbance in the <a href="/wiki/Second_Temple" title="Second Temple">Second Temple</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESanders199311_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESanders199311-37">[28]</a></sup> which was the center of Jewish religious and civil authority. Sanders associates it with Jesus' prophecy that the Temple would be totally demolished.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESanders1993254.E2.80.9362_326-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESanders1993254.E2.80.9362-326">[311]</a></sup> Jesus had a last meal with his disciples, which is the origin of the Christian sacrament of bread and wine. Jesus' words are recorded in the Synoptics and in Paul's <a href="/wiki/First_Epistle_to_the_Corinthians" title="First Epistle to the Corinthians">First Epistle to the Corinthians</a>. The differences in the accounts cannot be completely reconciled, and it is impossible to know what Jesus intended, but in general the meal seems to point forward to the coming Kingdom. Jesus probably expected to be killed, and he may have hoped that God would intervene.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESanders1993263.E2.80.9364_327-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESanders1993263.E2.80.9364-327">[312]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Gospels say that Jesus was betrayed to the authorities by a disciple, and many scholars consider this report to be highly reliable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrossLivingstone2005Jesus_Christ_146-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrossLivingstone2005Jesus_Christ-146">[134]</a></sup> He was executed on the orders of <a href="/wiki/Pontius_Pilate" title="Pontius Pilate">Pontius Pilate</a>, the Roman <a href="/wiki/Prefect" title="Prefect">prefect</a> of <a href="/wiki/Judea_(Roman_province)" title="Judea (Roman province)">Judaea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESanders199311_37-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESanders199311-37">[28]</a></sup> Pilate most likely saw Jesus' reference to the Kingdom of God as a threat to Roman authority and worked with the Temple elites to have Jesus executed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETheissenMerz1998465.E2.80.9366_328-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETheissenMerz1998465.E2.80.9366-328">[313]</a></sup> The Sadducean high-priestly leaders of the Temple more plausibly had Jesus executed for political reasons than for his teaching.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrossLivingstone2005Jesus_Christ_146-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrossLivingstone2005Jesus_Christ-146">[134]</a></sup> They may have regarded him as a threat to stability, especially after he caused a disturbance at the Second Temple.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrossLivingstone2005Jesus_Christ_146-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrossLivingstone2005Jesus_Christ-146">[134]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JE1906_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-JE1906-52">[42]</a></sup> Other factors, such as Jesus' triumphal entry into Jerusalem, may have contributed to this decision.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESanders1993269.E2.80.9373_329-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESanders1993269.E2.80.9373-329">[314]</a></sup> Most scholars consider Jesus' crucifixion to be factual, because early Christians would not have invented the painful death of their leader.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDunn2003339_9-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunn2003339-9">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeier2006126.E2.80.9328_330-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeier2006126.E2.80.9328-330">[315]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="After_crucifixion">After crucifixion</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Resurrection_Beaufort_arms_in_border_(f._131)_Cropped.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2417" data-file-width="1894" height="217" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/The_Resurrection_Beaufort_arms_in_border_%28f._131%29_Cropped.jpg/170px-The_Resurrection_Beaufort_arms_in_border_%28f._131%29_Cropped.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/The_Resurrection_Beaufort_arms_in_border_%28f._131%29_Cropped.jpg/255px-The_Resurrection_Beaufort_arms_in_border_%28f._131%29_Cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/The_Resurrection_Beaufort_arms_in_border_%28f._131%29_Cropped.jpg/340px-The_Resurrection_Beaufort_arms_in_border_%28f._131%29_Cropped.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Resurrection_Beaufort_arms_in_border_(f._131)_Cropped.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Resurrection of Christ from a 16th century copy of <i>La Passion de Nostre Seigneur</i></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After Jesus' death, his followers said he rose from the dead, although exact details of their experiences are unclear. Some of those who claimed to have witnessed Jesus' resurrection later died for their belief, which indicates that their beliefs were likely genuine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESanders1993_331-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESanders1993-331">[316]</a></sup> According to Sanders, the Gospel reports contradict each other, which, according to him, suggests competition among those claiming to have seen him first rather than deliberate fraud.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESanders1993276.E2.80.9381_332-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESanders1993276.E2.80.9381-332">[317]</a></sup> On the other hand, <a href="/wiki/L._Michael_White" title="L. Michael White">L. Michael White</a> suggests that inconsistencies in the Gospels reflect differences in the agendas of their unknown authors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-White_293-1"><a href="#cite_note-White-293">[278]</a></sup> The followers of Jesus formed a community to wait for his return and the founding of his kingdom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESanders199311_37-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESanders199311-37">[28]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Portraits_of_Jesus">Portraits of Jesus</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Historical_Jesus" title="Historical Jesus">Historical Jesus</a></div>
<p>Modern research on the historical Jesus has not led to a unified picture of the historical figure, partly because of the variety of academic traditions represented by the scholars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETheissenWinter20024.E2.80.935_333-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETheissenWinter20024.E2.80.935-333">[318]</a></sup> Given the scarcity of historical sources, it is generally difficult for any scholar to construct a portrait of Jesus that can be considered historically valid beyond the basic elements of his life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEK.C3.B6stenbergerKellumQuarles2009117.E2.80.9325_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEK.C3.B6stenbergerKellumQuarles2009117.E2.80.9325-98">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEhrman199922.E2.80.9323_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEhrman199922.E2.80.9323-99">[87]</a></sup> The portraits of Jesus constructed in these quests often differ from each other, and from the image portrayed in the gospels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETheissenWinter20025_334-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETheissenWinter20025-334">[319]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrossLivingstone2005Historical_Jesus.2C_Quest_of_the_335-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrossLivingstone2005Historical_Jesus.2C_Quest_of_the-335">[320]</a></sup></p>
<p>Jesus is seen as the founder of, in the words of Sanders, a '"renewal movement within Judaism." One of the criterion used to discern historical details in the "third quest" is the criterion of plausibility, relative to Jesus' Jewish context and to his influence on Christianity. A disagreement in contemporary research is whether Jesus was <a href="/wiki/Apocalypticism" title="Apocalypticism">apocalyptic</a>. Most scholars conclude that he was an apocalyptic preacher, like <a href="/wiki/John_the_Baptist" title="John the Baptist">John the Baptist</a> and <a href="/wiki/Paul_the_Apostle" title="Paul the Apostle">Paul the Apostle</a>. In contrast, certain prominent North American scholars, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Burton_Mack" title="Burton Mack">Burton Mack</a> and John Dominic Crossan, advocate for a non-eschatological Jesus, one who is more of a <a href="/wiki/Cynicism_(philosophy)" title="Cynicism (philosophy)">Cynic</a> <a href="/wiki/Sage_(philosophy)" title="Sage (philosophy)">sage</a> than an apocalyptic preacher.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETheissenMerz19981.E2.80.9315_336-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETheissenMerz19981.E2.80.9315-336">[321]</a></sup> In addition to portraying Jesus as an apocalyptic prophet, a charismatic healer or a <a href="/wiki/Cynicism_(philosophy)" title="Cynicism (philosophy)">cynic philosopher</a>, some scholars portray him as the true Messiah or an <a href="/wiki/Egalitarianism" title="Egalitarianism">egalitarian</a> prophet of <a href="/wiki/Social_change" title="Social change">social change</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CambHist23_337-0"><a href="#cite_note-CambHist23-337">[322]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEK.C3.B6stenbergerKellumQuarles2009124.E2.80.9325_338-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEK.C3.B6stenbergerKellumQuarles2009124.E2.80.9325-338">[323]</a></sup> However, the attributes described in the portraits sometimes overlap, and scholars who differ on some attributes sometimes agree on others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-339"><a href="#cite_note-339">[324]</a></sup></p>
<p>Since the 18th century, scholars have occasionally put forth that Jesus was a political national messiah, but the evidence for this portrait is negligible. Likewise, the proposal that Jesus was a <a href="/wiki/Zealots_(Judea)" title="Zealots (Judea)">Zealot</a> does not fit with the earliest strata of the Synoptic tradition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrossLivingstone2005Jesus_Christ_146-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrossLivingstone2005Jesus_Christ-146">[134]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="Language,_ethnicity,_and_appearance"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Language.2C_ethnicity.2C_and_appearance">Language, ethnicity, and appearance</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Language_of_Jesus" title="Language of Jesus">Language of Jesus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Race_and_appearance_of_Jesus" title="Race and appearance of Jesus">Race and appearance of Jesus</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CompositeJesus.JPG"><img alt="Twelve depictions of Jesus from around the world" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="398" data-file-width="272" height="249" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/CompositeJesus.JPG/170px-CompositeJesus.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/CompositeJesus.JPG/255px-CompositeJesus.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/65/CompositeJesus.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CompositeJesus.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The representation of the ethnicity of Jesus has been influenced by cultural settings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoulden200663.E2.80.9399_340-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoulden200663.E2.80.9399-340">[325]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Erricker44_341-0"><a href="#cite_note-Erricker44-341">[326]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Jesus grew up in Galilee and much of his ministry took place there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenMcKnightMarshall1992442_342-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenMcKnightMarshall1992442-342">[327]</a></sup> The languages spoken in Galilee and Judea during the 1st century AD include <a href="/wiki/Jewish_Palestinian_Aramaic" title="Jewish Palestinian Aramaic">Jewish Palestinian Aramaic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">Hebrew</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Koine_Greek" title="Koine Greek">Greek</a>, with Aramaic being predominant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BarrLang_343-0"><a href="#cite_note-BarrLang-343">[328]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Porter110_344-0"><a href="#cite_note-Porter110-344">[329]</a></sup> There is substantial consensus that Jesus gave most of his teachings in Aramaic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDunn2003313.E2.80.9315_345-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunn2003313.E2.80.9315-345">[330]</a></sup></p>
<p>Modern scholars agree that Jesus was a Jew of 1st century <a href="/wiki/Palestine_(region)" title="Palestine (region)">Palestine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEhrman199996_346-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEhrman199996-346">[331]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Celebrating_sacraments_347-0"><a href="#cite_note-Celebrating_sacraments-347">[332]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Ioudaios" title="Ioudaios">Ioudaios</a></i> in New Testament Greek<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-349"><a href="#cite_note-349">[p]</a></sup> is a term which in the contemporary context may refer to religion (<a href="/wiki/Second_Temple_Judaism" title="Second Temple Judaism">Second Temple Judaism</a>), ethnicity (of Judea), or both.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-350"><a href="#cite_note-350">[334]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-351"><a href="#cite_note-351">[335]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-352"><a href="#cite_note-352">[336]</a></sup> In a review of the state of modern scholarship, <a href="/wiki/Amy-Jill_Levine" title="Amy-Jill Levine">Amy-Jill Levine</a> writes that the entire question of ethnicity is "fraught with difficulty", and that "beyond recognizing that 'Jesus was Jewish', rarely does the scholarship address what being 'Jewish' means".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELevine200610_353-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELevine200610-353">[337]</a></sup></p>
<p>The New Testament gives no description of the physical appearance of Jesus before his death—it is generally indifferent to racial appearances and does not refer to the features of the people it mentions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-354"><a href="#cite_note-354">[338]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Perkinson30_355-0"><a href="#cite_note-Perkinson30-355">[339]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kidd48_356-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kidd48-356">[340]</a></sup> Jesus probably looked like a typical Jew of his time and according to some scholars was likely to have had a sinewy appearance due to <a href="/wiki/Homelessness_of_Jesus" title="Homelessness of Jesus">his ascetic and itinerant lifestyle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-357"><a href="#cite_note-357">[341]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Christ_myth_theory">Christ myth theory</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Christ_myth_theory" title="Christ myth theory">Christ myth theory</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Christ_myth_theory" title="Christ myth theory">Christ myth theory</a> is the hypothesis that Jesus of Nazareth never existed; or if he did, that he had virtually nothing to do with the founding of Christianity and the accounts in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gospels" title="Gospels">gospels</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-359"><a href="#cite_note-359">[q]</a></sup> Stories of Jesus' birth, along with other key events, have so many mythic elements that some scholars have suggested that Jesus himself was a <a href="/wiki/Myth" title="Myth">myth</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETheissenMerz1998113.E2.80.9315_360-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETheissenMerz1998113.E2.80.9315-360">[343]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bruno_Bauer" title="Bruno Bauer">Bruno Bauer</a> (1809–1882) taught that the first Gospel was a work of literature that produced history rather than described it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETheissenMerz199890_361-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETheissenMerz199890-361">[344]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Albert_Kalthoff" title="Albert Kalthoff">Albert Kalthoff</a> (1850–1906) a social movement produced Jesus when it encountered Jewish messianic expectations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETheissenMerz199890_361-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETheissenMerz199890-361">[344]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Drews" title="Arthur Drews">Arthur Drews</a> (1865–1935) saw Jesus as the concrete form of a myth that predated Christianity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETheissenMerz199890_361-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETheissenMerz199890-361">[344]</a></sup> Despite arguments put forward by authors who have questioned the existence of a <a href="/wiki/Historical_Jesus" title="Historical Jesus">historical Jesus</a>, there remains a strong consensus in <a href="/wiki/Historical_criticism" title="Historical criticism">historical-critical biblical scholarship</a> that a historical Jesus did live in that area and in that time period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DunnPaul35_362-0"><a href="#cite_note-DunnPaul35-362">[345]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Then.27_2004_page_34_363-0"><a href="#cite_note-Then.27_2004_page_34-363">[346]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jesus.27_page_200_364-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jesus.27_page_200-364">[347]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jesus.27_page_145_365-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jesus.27_page_145-365">[348]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-voorst16_366-0"><a href="#cite_note-voorst16-366">[349]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-367"><a href="#cite_note-367">[350]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-368"><a href="#cite_note-368">[351]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Perspectives">Perspectives</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Religious_perspectives_on_Jesus" title="Religious perspectives on Jesus">Religious perspectives on Jesus</a></div>
<p>Apart from his own disciples and followers, the Jews of Jesus' day generally rejected him as the Messiah, as do the great majority of Jews today. Christian theologians, <a href="/wiki/Ecumenical_council" title="Ecumenical council">ecumenical councils</a>, reformers and others have written extensively about Jesus over the centuries. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christian_sect" title="Christian sect">Christian sects</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christian_schisms" title="Christian schisms">schisms</a> have often been defined or characterized by their descriptions of Jesus. Meanwhile, <a href="/wiki/Manichaeism" title="Manichaeism">Manichaeans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gnosticism" title="Gnosticism">Gnostics</a>, Muslims, Baha'is, and others have found prominent places for Jesus in their religions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-369"><a href="#cite_note-369">[352]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-370"><a href="#cite_note-370">[353]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-371"><a href="#cite_note-371">[354]</a></sup> Jesus has also had detractors, both past and present.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Christian">Christian</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Jesus_in_Christianity" title="Jesus in Christianity">Jesus in Christianity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christ_(title)" title="Christ (title)">Christ (title)</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Christology" title="Christology">Christology</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Shield-Trinity-Scutum-Fidei-English.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="468" data-file-width="520" height="198" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Shield-Trinity-Scutum-Fidei-English.svg/220px-Shield-Trinity-Scutum-Fidei-English.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Shield-Trinity-Scutum-Fidei-English.svg/330px-Shield-Trinity-Scutum-Fidei-English.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Shield-Trinity-Scutum-Fidei-English.svg/440px-Shield-Trinity-Scutum-Fidei-English.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Shield-Trinity-Scutum-Fidei-English.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Trinity" title="Trinity">Trinity</a> is the belief in Christianity that God is one God in three persons: <a href="/wiki/God_the_Father" title="God the Father">God the Father</a>, <a href="/wiki/God_the_Son" title="God the Son">God the Son</a> (<a href="/wiki/Jesus_in_Christianity" title="Jesus in Christianity">Jesus</a>), and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Holy_Spirit_(Christianity)" title="Holy Spirit (Christianity)">God the Holy Spirit</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Christ_with_beard.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="558" height="237" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Christ_with_beard.jpg/220px-Christ_with_beard.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Christ_with_beard.jpg/330px-Christ_with_beard.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Christ_with_beard.jpg/440px-Christ_with_beard.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Christ_with_beard.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Jesus is depicted with the <a href="/wiki/Alpha_and_Omega" title="Alpha and Omega">Alpha and Omega</a> letters in the catacombs of Rome from the 4th century.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Jesus is the central figure of Christianity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcGrath20064.E2.80.936_19-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcGrath20064.E2.80.936-19">[13]</a></sup> Although Christian views of Jesus vary, it is possible to summarize the key beliefs shared among major <a href="/wiki/Christian_denomination" title="Christian denomination">denominations</a>, as stated in their <a href="/wiki/Catechism" title="Catechism">catechetical</a> or <a href="/wiki/Confessionalism_(religion)" title="Confessionalism (religion)">confessional</a> texts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-372"><a href="#cite_note-372">[355]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-373"><a href="#cite_note-373">[356]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-374"><a href="#cite_note-374">[357]</a></sup> Christian views of Jesus are derived from various sources, including the canonical gospels and New Testament letters such as the Pauline epistles and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Johannine_writings" title="Johannine writings">Johannine writings</a>. These documents outline the key beliefs held by Christians about Jesus, including his divinity, humanity, and earthly life, and that he is the Christ and the <a href="/wiki/Son_of_God_(Christianity)" title="Son of God (Christianity)">Son of God</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-375"><a href="#cite_note-375">[358]</a></sup> Despite their many shared beliefs, not all Christian denominations agree on all doctrines, and both <a href="/wiki/East%E2%80%93West_Schism" title="East–West Schism">major and minor differences</a> on teachings and beliefs have persisted throughout Christianity for centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrossLivingstone2005Great_Schism_376-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrossLivingstone2005Great_Schism-376">[359]</a></sup></p>
<p>The New Testament states that the resurrection of Jesus is the foundation of the Christian faith (<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(American_Standard)/1_Corinthians#15:12" title="s:Bible (American Standard)/1 Corinthians">1 Corinthians 15:12–20</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-377"><a href="#cite_note-377">[360]</a></sup> Christians believe that through his <a href="/wiki/Sacrifice#Christinity" title="Sacrifice">sacrificial</a> death and resurrection, humans can be <a href="/wiki/Reconciliation_(theology)" title="Reconciliation (theology)">reconciled with God</a> and are thereby offered <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salvation_(Christianity)" title="Salvation (Christianity)">salvation</a> and the promise of <a href="/wiki/Eternal_life_(Christianity)" title="Eternal life (Christianity)">eternal life</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oxford_Companion_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-Oxford_Companion-44">[34]</a></sup> Recalling the words of John the Baptist on the day after Jesus' baptism, these doctrines sometimes refer to Jesus as the Lamb of God, who was crucified to fulfill his role as the servant of God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-378"><a href="#cite_note-378">[361]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-379"><a href="#cite_note-379">[362]</a></sup> Jesus is thus seen as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Adam" title="New Adam">new and last Adam</a>, whose obedience contrasts with <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_man" title="Fall of man">Adam's disobedience</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-380"><a href="#cite_note-380">[363]</a></sup> Christians view Jesus as a role model, whose God-focused life believers are encouraged to imitate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcGrath20064.E2.80.936_19-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcGrath20064.E2.80.936-19">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>Most Christians believe that Jesus was both human and the Son of God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ehrman_Jesus_became_God_20-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ehrman_Jesus_became_God-20">[14]</a></sup> While there has been <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christological_controversies" title="Christological controversies">theological debate</a> over his nature,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-381"><a href="#cite_note-381">[r]</a></sup> Some early Christians viewed Jesus as subordinate to the Father, and others considered him an aspect of the Father rather than a separate person.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_53-11"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-53">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrossLivingstone2005Antitrinitarianism_382-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrossLivingstone2005Antitrinitarianism-382">[364]</a></sup> The Church resolved the issues in ancient councils, which established the Holy Trinity, with Jesus both fully human and fully God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_53-12"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-53">[43]</a></sup> Trinitarian Christians generally believe that Jesus is the Logos, God's incarnation and <a href="/wiki/God_the_Son" title="God the Son">God the Son</a>, both fully divine and fully human. However, the doctrine of the Trinity is not universally accepted among Christians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-383"><a href="#cite_note-383">[365]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-384"><a href="#cite_note-384">[366]</a></sup> With the Protestant Reformation, Christians such as <a href="/wiki/Michael_Servetus" title="Michael Servetus">Michael Servetus</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Socinian" title="Socinian">Socinians</a> started questioning the ancient creeds that had established Jesus' two natures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_53-13"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-53">[43]</a></sup> Nontrinitarian Christian groups include <a href="/wiki/The_Church_of_Jesus_Christ_of_Latter-day_Saints" title="The Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints">The Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-385"><a href="#cite_note-385">[367]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Unitarianism" title="Unitarianism">Unitarians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jehovah%27s_Witnesses" title="Jehovah's Witnesses">Jehovah's Witnesses</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrossLivingstone2005Antitrinitarianism_382-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrossLivingstone2005Antitrinitarianism-382">[364]</a></sup></p>
<p>Christians revere not only Jesus himself, but also his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Name_of_Jesus" title="Name of Jesus">name</a>. Devotions to the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Name_of_Jesus" title="Holy Name of Jesus">Holy Name of Jesus</a> go back to the earliest days of Christianity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-386"><a href="#cite_note-386">[368]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoulden2006426_387-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoulden2006426-387">[369]</a></sup> These devotions and feasts exist in both <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Christianity" title="Eastern Christianity">Eastern</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_Church" title="Western Church">Western Christianity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoulden2006426_387-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoulden2006426-387">[369]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Jewish">Jewish</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Judaism%27s_view_of_Jesus" title="Judaism's view of Jesus">Judaism's view of Jesus</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Jesus_in_the_Talmud" title="Jesus in the Talmud">Jesus in the Talmud</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Judaism</a> rejects the idea of Jesus being God,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JE1906_52-2"><a href="#cite_note-JE1906-52">[42]</a></sup> or a mediator to God, or part of a Trinity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-388"><a href="#cite_note-388">[370]</a></sup> It holds that Jesus is not the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jewish_Messianism" title="Jewish Messianism">Messiah</a>, arguing that he neither fulfilled the Messianic prophecies in the <a href="/wiki/Tanakh" title="Tanakh">Tanakh</a> nor embodied the personal qualifications of the Messiah.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-389"><a href="#cite_note-389">[371]</a></sup> Jews argue that Jesus did not fulfill prophesies to build the <a href="/wiki/Third_Temple" title="Third Temple">Third Temple</a> (<a class="external text" href="http://www.mechon-mamre.org/p/pt/pt1237.htm#26" rel="nofollow">Ezekiel 37:26–28</a>), gather Jews back to Israel (<a class="external text" href="http://www.mechon-mamre.org/p/pt/pt1043.htm#5" rel="nofollow">Isaiah 43:5–6</a>), bring world peace (<a class="external text" href="http://www.mechon-mamre.org/p/pt/pt1002.htm#4" rel="nofollow">Isaiah 2:4</a>), and unite humanity under the God of Israel (<a class="external text" href="http://www.mechon-mamre.org/p/pt/pt2314.htm#9" rel="nofollow">Zechariah 14:9</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-simmons_390-0"><a href="#cite_note-simmons-390">[372]</a></sup> Furthermore, according to Jewish tradition, there were no prophets after <a href="/wiki/Malachi" title="Malachi">Malachi</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-391"><a href="#cite_note-391">[373]</a></sup> who delivered his prophesies in the 5th century BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-392"><a href="#cite_note-392">[374]</a></sup></p>
<p>Judaic criticism of Jesus is long-standing. The Talmud, written and compiled from the 3rd to the 5th century AD,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-393"><a href="#cite_note-393">[375]</a></sup> includes <a href="/wiki/Jesus_in_the_Talmud" title="Jesus in the Talmud">stories</a> that since medieval times have been considered to be defamatory accounts of Jesus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-394"><a href="#cite_note-394">[376]</a></sup> In one such story, <i>Yeshu HaNozri</i> ("Jesus the Christian"), a lewd apostate, is executed by the Jewish high court for spreading idolatry and practicing magic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-395"><a href="#cite_note-395">[377]</a></sup> The majority of contemporary scholars consider that this material provides no information on the historical Jesus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETheissenMerz199874.E2.80.9375_396-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETheissenMerz199874.E2.80.9375-396">[378]</a></sup> The <i><a href="/wiki/Mishneh_Torah" title="Mishneh Torah">Mishneh Torah</a></i>, a late 12th century work of <a href="/wiki/Halakha" title="Halakha">Jewish law</a> written by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moses_Maimonides" title="Moses Maimonides">Moses Maimonides</a>, states that Jesus is a "stumbling block" who makes "the majority of the world to err and serve a god other than the Lord".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-397"><a href="#cite_note-397">[379]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Islamic">Islamic</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Jesus_in_Islam" title="Jesus in Islam">Jesus in Islam</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:216px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jesus_Name_in_Arabic.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="208" data-file-width="214" height="208" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/19/Jesus_Name_in_Arabic.gif" width="214"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jesus_Name_in_Arabic.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The name <i>Jesus son of Mary</i> written in <a href="/wiki/Islamic_calligraphy" title="Islamic calligraphy">Islamic calligraphy</a> followed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peace_be_upon_him_(Islam)" title="Peace be upon him (Islam)">Peace be upon him</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A major figure in Islam,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-uscsite_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-uscsite-48">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Siddiqui_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-Siddiqui-50">[40]</a></sup> Jesus (commonly transliterated as <i>ʾĪsā</i>) is considered to be a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apostle_(Islam)" title="Apostle (Islam)">messenger</a> of <a href="/wiki/God_in_Islam" title="God in Islam">God</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Allah" title="Allah">Allah</a></i>) and the Messiah (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Messiah_in_Islam" title="Messiah in Islam">al-Masih</a></i>) who was sent to guide the <a href="/wiki/Israelites" title="Israelites">Children of Israel</a> (<i>Bani Isra'il</i>) with a new scripture, the Gospel (referred to in Islam as <i><a href="/wiki/Gospel_in_Islam" title="Gospel in Islam">Injil</a></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CEI_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-CEI-49">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-398"><a href="#cite_note-398">[380]</a></sup> Muslims regard the gospels of the New Testament as inauthentic, and believe that Jesus' original message was <a href="/wiki/Tahrif" title="Tahrif">lost or altered</a> and that <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_in_Islam" title="Muhammad in Islam">Muhammad</a> came later to restore it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-399"><a href="#cite_note-399">[381]</a></sup> Belief in Jesus (and all other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prophets_in_Islam" title="Prophets in Islam">messengers of God</a>) is a requirement for being a <a href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">Muslim</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-400"><a href="#cite_note-400">[382]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Jesus_in_Islam" title="Jesus in Islam">Quran mentions Jesus</a> by name 25 times—more often than Muhammad<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-401"><a href="#cite_note-401">[383]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-402"><a href="#cite_note-402">[384]</a></sup>—and emphasizes that Jesus was a mortal human who, like all other prophets, had been divinely chosen to spread God's message.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-comparative_403-0"><a href="#cite_note-comparative-403">[385]</a></sup> While the Qur'an acknowledges the Virgin birth of Jesus, he is considered to be neither the incarnation nor the <a href="/wiki/Son_of_God_(Christianity)" title="Son of God (Christianity)">son of God</a>. Islamic texts emphasize a strict notion of <a href="/wiki/Monotheism" title="Monotheism">monotheism</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Tawhid" title="Tawhid">tawhid</a></i>) and forbid the association of partners with God, which would be <a href="/wiki/Shirk_(Islam)" title="Shirk (Islam)">idolatry</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-404"><a href="#cite_note-404">[386]</a></sup> Like all <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prophets_in_Islam" title="Prophets in Islam">prophets in Islam</a>, Jesus is considered a Muslim.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-405"><a href="#cite_note-405">[387]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Quran describes the annunciation to Mary (<i><a href="/wiki/Mary_in_Islam" title="Mary in Islam">Maryam</a></i>) by an angel that she is to give birth to Jesus while remaining a virgin. It calls the virgin birth a miracle that occurred by the will of God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RobB32_406-0"><a href="#cite_note-RobB32-406">[388]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peters23_407-0"><a href="#cite_note-Peters23-407">[389]</a></sup> The Quran (21:91 and 66:12) states that God breathed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Holy_Spirit_(Islam)" title="Holy Spirit (Islam)">his spirit</a> into Mary while she was chaste.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RobB32_406-1"><a href="#cite_note-RobB32-406">[388]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peters23_407-1"><a href="#cite_note-Peters23-407">[389]</a></sup> Jesus is called the "Spirit of God" because he was born through the action of the Spirit,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RobB32_406-2"><a href="#cite_note-RobB32-406">[388]</a></sup> but that belief does not imply <a href="/wiki/Pre-existence_of_Christ" title="Pre-existence of Christ">his pre-existence</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-408"><a href="#cite_note-408">[390]</a></sup></p>
<p>To aid in his ministry to the Jewish people, Jesus was given the ability to perform <a href="/wiki/Miracle" title="Miracle">miracles</a>, by permission of God rather than by his own power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morgan_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-Morgan-51">[41]</a></sup> Through his ministry, Jesus is seen as a <a href="/wiki/Precursor_(religion)" title="Precursor (religion)">precursor</a> to Muhammad.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-comparative_403-1"><a href="#cite_note-comparative-403">[385]</a></sup> According to the Quran, Jesus was not crucified but was merely made to appear that way to unbelievers by Allah,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-409"><a href="#cite_note-409">[391]</a></sup> who physically raised Jesus into the heavens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cite_quran.7C4.7C157.7Cstyle.3Dref_410-0"><a href="#cite_note-cite_quran.7C4.7C157.7Cstyle.3Dref-410">[392]</a></sup> To Muslims, it is the ascension rather than the crucifixion that constitutes a major event in the life of Jesus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Khalidi_411-0"><a href="#cite_note-Khalidi-411">[393]</a></sup> Most Muslims believe that Jesus will return to earth at the <a href="/wiki/End_time#Islam" title="End time">end of time</a> and defeat the <a href="/wiki/Antichrist" title="Antichrist">Antichrist</a> (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Masih_ad-Dajjal" title="Masih ad-Dajjal">ad-Dajjal</a></i>) by killing him in Lud.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CEI_49-2"><a href="#cite_note-CEI-49">[39]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Ahmadiyya" title="Ahmadiyya">Ahmadiyya Muslim Community</a> has several <a href="/wiki/Jesus_in_Ahmadiyya_Islam" title="Jesus in Ahmadiyya Islam">distinct teachings</a> about Jesus. Ahmadis believe that he was a mortal man who survived his crucifixion and died a natural death at the age of 120 in <a href="/wiki/Kashmir" title="Kashmir">Kashmir</a>, India and is buried at <a href="/wiki/Roza_Bal" title="Roza Bal">Roza Bal</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Melton55_412-0"><a href="#cite_note-Melton55-412">[394]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="Bahá'í"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Bah.C3.A1.27.C3.AD">Bahá'í</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Bah%C3%A1%27%C3%AD_Faith" title="Bahá'í Faith">Bahá'í</a> teachings consider Jesus to be a <a href="/wiki/Manifestation_of_God" title="Manifestation of God">manifestation of God</a>, a Bahá'í concept for prophets<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stockman_413-0"><a href="#cite_note-stockman-413">[395]</a></sup>—intermediaries between God and humanity, serving as messengers and reflecting God's qualities and attributes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manifestation_414-0"><a href="#cite_note-manifestation-414">[396]</a></sup> The Bahá'í concept emphasizes the simultaneous qualities of humanity and divinity;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manifestation_414-1"><a href="#cite_note-manifestation-414">[396]</a></sup> thus, it is similar to the Christian concept of incarnation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stockman_413-1"><a href="#cite_note-stockman-413">[395]</a></sup> Bahá'í thought accepts Jesus as the Son of God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith-jesus_415-0"><a href="#cite_note-Smith-jesus-415">[397]</a></sup> In Bahá'í thought, Jesus was a perfect incarnation of God's attributes, but Bahá'í teachings reject the idea that "ineffable essence" of the Divinity was contained within a single human body because of their beliefs regarding "<a href="/wiki/Omnipresence" title="Omnipresence">omnipresence</a> and <a href="/wiki/Transcendence_(religion)#Bah.C3.A1.27.C3.AD_Faith" title="Transcendence (religion)">transcendence</a> of the essence of God".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stockman_413-2"><a href="#cite_note-stockman-413">[395]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Bah%C3%A1%27u%27ll%C3%A1h" title="Bahá'u'lláh">Bahá'u'lláh</a>, the founder of the Bahá'í Faith, wrote that since each manifestation of God has the same divine attributes, they can be seen as the spiritual "return" of all previous manifestations of God, and the appearance of each new manifestation of God inaugurates a religion that supersedes the former ones, a concept known as <a href="/wiki/Progressive_revelation_(Bah%C3%A1%27%C3%AD)" title="Progressive revelation (Bahá'í)">progressive revelation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manifestation_414-2"><a href="#cite_note-manifestation-414">[396]</a></sup> Bahá'ís believe that God's plan unfolds gradually through this process as mankind matures, and that some of the manifestations arrive in specific fulfillment of the missions of previous ones. Thus, Bahá'ís believe that Bahá'u'lláh is the promised return of Christ.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psmith128_416-0"><a href="#cite_note-psmith128-416">[398]</a></sup> Bahá'í teachings confirm many, but not all, aspects of Jesus as portrayed in the gospels. Bahá'ís believe in the virgin birth and in the Crucifixion,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lepart118_417-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lepart118-417">[399]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cole_418-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cole-418">[400]</a></sup> but see the Resurrection and the miracles of Jesus as symbolic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith-jesus_415-1"><a href="#cite_note-Smith-jesus-415">[397]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cole_418-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cole-418">[400]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other">Other</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Criticism_of_Jesus" title="Criticism of Jesus">Criticism of Jesus</a></div>
<p>In Christian Gnosticism (now a largely extinct religious movement),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-419"><a href="#cite_note-419">[401]</a></sup> Jesus was sent from the divine realm and provided the secret knowledge (<a href="/wiki/Gnosis" title="Gnosis">gnosis</a>) necessary for salvation. Most Gnostics believed that Jesus was a human who became possessed by the spirit of "the Christ" at his baptism. This spirit left Jesus' body during the crucifixion, but was rejoined to him when he was raised from the dead. Some Gnostics, however, were <a href="/wiki/Docetism" title="Docetism">docetics</a>, believed that Jesus did not have a physical body, but only appeared to possess one.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-420"><a href="#cite_note-420">[402]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Manichaeism" title="Manichaeism">Manichaeism</a>, a Gnostic sect, accepted Jesus as a prophet, in addition to revering <a href="/wiki/Gautama_Buddha" title="Gautama Buddha">Gautama Buddha</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zoroaster" title="Zoroaster">Zoroaster</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-421"><a href="#cite_note-421">[403]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-422"><a href="#cite_note-422">[404]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hindus</a> consider Jesus to be an <a href="/wiki/Avatar" title="Avatar">avatar</a> or a <a href="/wiki/Sadhu" title="Sadhu">sadhu</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-423"><a href="#cite_note-423">[405]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Paramahansa_Yogananda" title="Paramahansa Yogananda">Paramahansa Yogananda</a>, an Indian <a href="/wiki/Guru" title="Guru">guru</a>, taught that Jesus was the reincarnation of <a href="/wiki/Elisha" title="Elisha">Elisha</a> and a student of <a href="/wiki/John_the_Baptist" title="John the Baptist">John the Baptist</a>, the reincarnation of <a href="/wiki/Elijah" title="Elijah">Elijah</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-424"><a href="#cite_note-424">[406]</a></sup> Some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buddhist" title="Buddhist">Buddhists</a>, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tenzin_Gyatso,_14th_Dalai_Lama" title="Tenzin Gyatso, 14th Dalai Lama">Tenzin Gyatso, the 14th Dalai Lama</a>, regard Jesus as a <a href="/wiki/Bodhisattva" title="Bodhisattva">bodhisattva</a> who dedicated his life to the welfare of people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-425"><a href="#cite_note-425">[407]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/New_Age" title="New Age">New Age</a> movement entertains a wide variety of views on Jesus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-other_views_426-0"><a href="#cite_note-other_views-426">[408]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Theosophy" title="Theosophy">Theosophists</a>, from whom many New Age teachings originated,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-427"><a href="#cite_note-427">[409]</a></sup> refer to Jesus as the <a href="/wiki/Master_Jesus" title="Master Jesus">Master Jesus</a> and believe that Christ, after <a href="/wiki/Reincarnation" title="Reincarnation">various incarnations</a>, occupied the body of Jesus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-428"><a href="#cite_note-428">[410]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jesus_in_Scientology" title="Jesus in Scientology">Scientologists recognize</a> Jesus (along with other religious figures such as Zoroaster, <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a>, and Buddha) as part of their "religious heritage".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-other_views_426-1"><a href="#cite_note-other_views-426">[408]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-429"><a href="#cite_note-429">[411]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atheist" title="Atheist">Atheists</a> reject Jesus' divinity, but have differing views on Jesus' moral teachings. For example, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Dawkins" title="Richard Dawkins">Richard Dawkins</a> has called him as "a great moral teacher",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-430"><a href="#cite_note-430">[412]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-431"><a href="#cite_note-431">[413]</a></sup> while <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Hitchens" title="Christopher Hitchens">Christopher Hitchens</a> deemed his teachings on forgiveness "positively immoral".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hitchens2001_432-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hitchens2001-432">[414]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Artistic_depictions">Artistic depictions</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Depiction_of_Jesus" title="Depiction of Jesus">Depiction of Jesus</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dura-europos-paralytic.jpg"><img alt="An ancient wall painting depicting Jesus" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="527" data-file-width="333" height="269" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Dura-europos-paralytic.jpg/170px-Dura-europos-paralytic.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Dura-europos-paralytic.jpg/255px-Dura-europos-paralytic.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b0/Dura-europos-paralytic.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dura-europos-paralytic.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Jesus healing a paralytic in one of the first known images of Jesus from <a href="/wiki/Dura-Europos_synagogue" title="Dura-Europos synagogue">Dura Europos</a> in the 2nd century<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-433"><a href="#cite_note-433">[415]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Some of the earliest depictions of Jesus at the <a href="/wiki/Dura-Europos_church" title="Dura-Europos church">Dura-Europos church</a> are firmly dated to before 256.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-434"><a href="#cite_note-434">[416]</a></sup> Thereafter, despite the lack of biblical references or historical records, a wide range of depictions of Jesus appeared during the last two millennia, often influenced by cultural settings, political circumstances and theological contexts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoulden200663.E2.80.9399_340-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoulden200663.E2.80.9399-340">[325]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Erricker44_341-1"><a href="#cite_note-Erricker44-341">[326]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Perkinson30_355-1"><a href="#cite_note-Perkinson30-355">[339]</a></sup> As in other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_Christian_art" title="Early Christian art">Early Christian art</a>, the earliest depictions date to the late 2nd or early 3rd century, and surviving images are found especially in the <a href="/wiki/Catacombs_of_Rome" title="Catacombs of Rome">Catacombs of Rome</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-435"><a href="#cite_note-435">[417]</a></sup></p>
<p>The depiction of Christ in pictorial form was highly <a href="/wiki/Aniconism_in_Christianity" title="Aniconism in Christianity">controversial</a> in the early church.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-phschaff_436-0"><a href="#cite_note-phschaff-436">[418]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-437"><a href="#cite_note-437">[s]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Elvira36_438-0"><a href="#cite_note-Elvira36-438">[419]</a></sup> From the 5th century onward, flat painted icons became popular in the Eastern Church.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrossLivingstone2005Icons_439-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrossLivingstone2005Icons-439">[420]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Iconoclasm" title="Byzantine Iconoclasm">Byzantine Iconoclasm</a> acted as a barrier to developments in the East, but by the ninth century, art was permitted again.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoulden200663.E2.80.9399_340-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoulden200663.E2.80.9399-340">[325]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformation" title="Protestant Reformation">Protestant Reformation</a> brought renewed <a href="/wiki/Aniconism_in_Christianity" title="Aniconism in Christianity">resistance to imagery</a>, but total prohibition was atypical, and Protestant objections to images have tended to reduce since the 16th century. Although large images are generally avoided, few Protestants now object to book illustrations depicting Jesus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-440"><a href="#cite_note-440">[421]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-441"><a href="#cite_note-441">[422]</a></sup> The use of depictions of Jesus is advocated by the leaders of denominations such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anglicans" title="Anglicans">Anglicans</a> and Catholics<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RWilliams83_442-0"><a href="#cite_note-RWilliams83-442">[423]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-443"><a href="#cite_note-443">[424]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-444"><a href="#cite_note-444">[425]</a></sup> and is a key element of the Eastern Orthodox tradition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoninger1999231_445-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoninger1999231-445">[426]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-446"><a href="#cite_note-446">[427]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Transfiguration_of_Jesus_in_Christian_art" title="Transfiguration of Jesus in Christian art">Transfiguration</a> was a major theme in Eastern Christian art, and every <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox" title="Eastern Orthodox">Eastern Orthodox</a> monk who had trained in <a href="/wiki/Icon" title="Icon">icon</a> painting had to prove his craft by painting an icon depicting it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-447"><a href="#cite_note-447">[428]</a></sup> Icons receive the external marks of veneration, such as kisses and prostration, and they are thought to be powerful channels of divine grace.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrossLivingstone2005Icons_439-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrossLivingstone2005Icons-439">[420]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> brought forth a number of artists who focused on depictions of Jesus; <a href="/wiki/Fra_Angelico" title="Fra Angelico">Fra Angelico</a> and others followed <a href="/wiki/Giotto" title="Giotto">Giotto</a> in the systematic development of uncluttered images.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoulden200663.E2.80.9399_340-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoulden200663.E2.80.9399-340">[325]</a></sup></p>
<p>Before the Protestant Reformation, the <a href="/wiki/Crucifix" title="Crucifix">crucifix</a> was common in Western Christianity. It is a model of the cross with Jesus crucified on it. The crucifix became the central ornament of the altar in the 13th century, a use that has been nearly universal in Roman Catholic churches until recent times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrossLivingstone2005Crucifix_448-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrossLivingstone2005Crucifix-448">[429]</a></sup></p>
<p>Jesus appears as an infant in a manger (feed trough) in Christmas creches, which depict the <a href="/wiki/Nativity_scene" title="Nativity scene">Nativity scene</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EB16032015_449-0"><a href="#cite_note-EB16032015-449">[430]</a></sup> He is typically joined by Mary, Joseph, animals, shepherds, angels, and the <a href="/wiki/Biblical_Magi" title="Biblical Magi">Magi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EB16032015_449-1"><a href="#cite_note-EB16032015-449">[430]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Francis_of_Assisi" title="Francis of Assisi">Francis of Assisi</a> (1181/82–1226) is credited with popularizing the creche, although he probably did not initiate it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EB16032015_449-2"><a href="#cite_note-EB16032015-449">[430]</a></sup> The creche reached its height of popularity in the 17th and 18th centuries in southern Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EB16032015_449-3"><a href="#cite_note-EB16032015-449">[430]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Associated_relics">Associated relics</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Relics_associated_with_Jesus" title="Relics associated with Jesus">Relics associated with Jesus</a></div>
<p>The total destruction that ensued with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Siege_of_Jerusalem_(70)" title="Siege of Jerusalem (70)">siege of Jerusalem</a> by the Romans in AD 70 made the survival of items from 1st century Judea very rare and almost no direct records survive about the history of Judaism from the last part of the 1st century through the 2nd century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELevine200624.E2.80.9325_450-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELevine200624.E2.80.9325-450">[431]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Koester382_451-0"><a href="#cite_note-Koester382-451">[432]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-453"><a href="#cite_note-453">[t]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Margaret_M._Mitchell" title="Margaret M. Mitchell">Margaret M. Mitchell</a> writes that although <a href="/wiki/Eusebius" title="Eusebius">Eusebius</a> reports (<i><a href="/wiki/Church_History_(Eusebius)" title="Church History (Eusebius)">Ecclesiastical History</a></i> III 5.3) that the early Christians left Jerusalem for <a href="/wiki/Pella,_Jordan" title="Pella, Jordan">Pella</a> just before Jerusalem was subjected to the final lock down, we must accept that no first hand Christian items from the early Jerusalem Church have reached us.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-454"><a href="#cite_note-454">[434]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Joe_Nickell" title="Joe Nickell">Joe Nickell</a> writes, "as investigation after investigation has shown, not a single, reliably authenticated relic of Jesus exists."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-455"><a href="#cite_note-455">[435]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-458"><a href="#cite_note-458">[u]</a></sup></p>
<p>However, throughout the history of Christianity a number of <a href="/wiki/Relic" title="Relic">relics</a> attributed to Jesus have been claimed, although doubt has been cast on them. The 16th century Catholic theologian <a href="/wiki/Erasmus" title="Erasmus">Erasmus</a> wrote sarcastically about the proliferation of relics and the number of buildings that could have been constructed from the wood claimed to be from the <a href="/wiki/True_Cross" title="True Cross">cross used in the Crucifixion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDillenberger19995_459-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDillenberger19995-459">[438]</a></sup> Similarly, while experts debate whether Jesus was crucified with three nails or with four, at least thirty <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Holy_nail" title="Holy nail">holy nails</a> continue to be venerated as relics across Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-460"><a href="#cite_note-460">[439]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some relics, such as purported remnants of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crown_of_Thorns" title="Crown of Thorns">Crown of Thorns</a>, receive only a modest number of pilgrims, while the <a href="/wiki/Shroud_of_Turin" title="Shroud of Turin">Shroud of Turin</a> (which is associated with an approved <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catholic_devotion" title="Catholic devotion">Catholic devotion</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Face_of_Jesus" title="Holy Face of Jesus">Holy Face of Jesus</a>), has received millions,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-461"><a href="#cite_note-461">[440]</a></sup> including <a href="/wiki/Pope" title="Pope">popes</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_Paul_II" title="John Paul II">John Paul II</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pope_Benedict_XVI" title="Pope Benedict XVI">Benedict XVI</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-462"><a href="#cite_note-462">[441]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-463"><a href="#cite_note-463">[442]</a></sup></p>
